FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Butyriboletus abieticola  (Thiers) D. Arora & J.L Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 466. 2014;  Boletus abieticola  Thiers  California Mushrooms pp.39-40. 1975
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a cap that is light rose with tan-colored spots interspersed and appearing yellow under shingled fibrillose scales, 2) white to pale yellow flesh that occasionally changes to blue, 3) yellow tubes and pores that change to blue, 4) yellow stem with reddish base and yellow reticulation on upper part, and 5) microscopic characters including spirally incrusted hyphae of the cap cuticle.^ According to Bessette(3), it is fairly common. RANGE Butyriboletus abieticola is found from the Rocky Mountains to the Pacific Northwest and CA, (Bessette). There are collections from WA at the University of Washington and from OR at Oregon State University.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^KOH on flesh pale violaceous, (Thiers)
CAP  9-13cm, convex becoming flat-convex at maturity, margin incurved, entire; "light rose with tan-colored spots interspersed and appearing yellow under the scales"; "dry, appressed fibrillose, forming more or less imbricated fibrillose scales that remain apparent even when dried"^, (Thiers), 5-13cm wide, convex, becoming broadly convex to flat when old, margin incurved and even; "predominantly pinkish to tan on a yellowish ground color"; dry, appressed-fibrillose to fibrillose-scaly, (Bessette)
FLESH  up to 2cm thick; "white to pale yellow, usually unchanging, occasionally slowly changing to blue in irregular areas when exposed"; in stem white except reddish at base, unchanging when exposed^, (Thiers), white to yellowish, unchanging or slowly turning blue erratically, in stem white except for reddish base, unchanging when cut, (Bessette)
PORES  up to 1mm, angular; yellow ("lemon yellow") when young, darkening and with greenish tints when mature, changing to blue when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, "adnate to adnexed, shallowly depressed with age", colored as pores, changing to blue when bruised or exposed^, (Thiers), pores up to 0.1cm wide, pore surface "yellow at first, becoming olive-yellow at maturity, staining blue when bruised", tubes 1-2cm deep, often depressed near stem when old, (Bessette)
STEM  9-12cm x 4-5cm, widening to 5-6cm at widest point, club-shaped to somewhat bulbous, solid; yellow, reddish at base; strongly yellow-reticulate over all but basal part^, (Thiers), 4-12cm x 2.5-5cm, widening downwards, solid; yellow with reddish base, dry, strongly yellow reticulate at least on upper part, often nearly over the whole surface; partial veil and ring absent blueing, (Bessette)
ODOR  mild^ (Thiers), not distinctive (Bessette)
TASTE  mild, sweet^, (Thiers), not distinctive (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Thiers), yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  "scattered or in groups on the ground in conifer forests, especially firs, at higher elevations, August-January"^, (Bessette), rare at lower elevations but not uncommon in the zone characterized by Abies lasiocarpa (Subalpine Fir) / Tsuga mertensiana (Mountain Hemlock), (R. Bishop, pers. comm.), summer, fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-17.5 x 4.5-5.5 microns, elliptic to somewhat spindle-shaped to somewhat cylindric in face view, inequilateral and wider in middle in side view, smooth, thin-walled^, pale ochraceous in KOH, ochraceous in Melzer's; basidia 1-, 2-, and 4-spored, 22-27 x 8-10 microns, clavate, colorless, guttulate in KOH, hymenium colorless with scattered yellow areas in KOH, bright rust-red color in Melzer's; hymenial cystidia scattered to rare, inconspicuous, 40-50 x 4-7 microns, cylindric to pyriform (pear-shaped) to subclavate to subfusoid, colorless in KOH and Melzer's, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a tangled trichodermium of hyphae with occasional erect hyphal tips, hyaline spiral incrustations on most hyphae, contents staining bright red to tawny in Melzer's, hyphae 8-10 microns wide"; stem cuticle composed largely of fertile basidia; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 14-18 x 4.5-6 microns, elliptic to subcylindric, smooth, pale ochraceous, (Bessette)
NAME ORIGIN  means "dwelling with Abies [fir]"
SIMILAR  Butyriboletus persolidus (established for California) differs in cap color, does not show the imbricated fibrillose scales on cap, is not noted to be associated with fir, and has slightly smaller spores and differently shaped cystidia, (Thiers). See also SIMILAR section of Butyriboletus autumniregius, Butyriboletus primiregius, and Butyriboletus querciregius.
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus abieticola), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus abieticola), Arora(3), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus abieticola), Siegel(2)*
17
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Butyriboletus autumniregius  D. Arora & J.L. Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 475. 2014;  Boletus regius  Krombh.  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) large size, 2) cap that is rose red or vinaceous red or at times mottled with tan or yellow, dry, and bald or breaking up into small scales, 3) flesh that is yellow but vinaceous tinged in the stem base, 4) small yellow pores that become greenish, and turn blue when bruised, 5) bulbous to equal stem that is yellow with dark red stains toward base, and yellow-reticulate in upper part, often staining blue when bruised or rubbed, 6) fruiting under conifers and possibly hardwoods, generally in the fall, in mountains or lowlands, and 7) spores with length to width ratio 3.5. The descriptions is derived from Arora(3). RANGE Butyriboletus autumniregius collections were examined from CA and OR.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  8-30cm across, convex becoming broadly convex when mature or sometimes flat; "wholly or mostly rose red to vinaceous red or at times mottled with tan or yellow"; dry or slightly tacky but not viscid, bald or "breaking up to form many small scales" (the scales typically smaller than in Butyriboletus abieticola)
FLESH  firm, dense, thick; "pale yellow, typically unchanging in the button stage but in age usually turning blue at least somewhat when cut (often slowly and erratically)"; in stem, "firm, pale yellow except for the base, which usually has a distinct vinaceous tinge, turning blue slightly or not at all when cut"
PORES  typically less than 1 millimeter wide, pore surface usually the same color as the tubes which are "pale yellow becoming bright yellow and eventually olive yellow or greenish", pore surface "typically turning blue quickly when bruised except in the button stage"; tubes at first short in relation to the cap flesh but 1-2.5cm or longer when mature
STEM  5-15(25)cm x 3-7cm at top, usually clavate [club-shaped] "and sometimes markedly bulbous when young, but often less so (at times equal) with age"; "butter yellow to bright yellow throughout or commonly with dark red (rhubarb red) stains toward the base, often bruising blue when touched or rubbed"; finely reticulate over at least upper part, "the reticulation yellow or occasionally reddish but often becoming brown after spores are shed"
ODOR  
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or in groups on the ground in coastal and mid-elevation forests, "associated with conifers and possibly also with hardwoods", has been observed in pure stands of Douglas-fir; fruiting generally in the fall (September to December)
SPORE DEPOSIT  spores dark olive brown in mass, at least under microscope
MICROSCOPIC  spores (12)13-16.5(18) x 4-5 microns (length to width ratio 3.5), fusoid, smooth, thin-walled, "pale brown or yellowish in KOH"^; basidia 4-spored, clavate; "hymenial cystidia fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle "an interwoven trichodermium with few erect hyphal tips"
NAME ORIGIN  "autumni" refers to autumn fruiting, and "regius" means "kingly" and also refers to the European Butyriboletus regius
SIMILAR  Butyriboletus primiregius is best distinguished by fruiting in the spring. The cap has a pronounced tendency, lacking in B. autumniregius, to fade to dingy olive brown or paler when it is exposed to sunlight, but this could be environmental. Butyriboletus querciregius 1) grows with oak, 2) has shorter spores and a smaller length to width ratio (3.0), and 3) has a less vivid cap color (pinkish red, pinkish brown, or yellowish when fresh as opposed to tending toward deep rose red or vinaceous red when fresh). The cap color of the cap of B. querciregius is also referred to as "red or dull red or even orange-red, sometimes blotched with yellow before fading to pinkish brown, tan or yellowish". Butyriboletus regius, known from Europe turns blue only rarely when cut. Butyriboletus abieticola has fibrillose scales when mature. (Arora(3)).
SOURCES  Arora(3)*
18
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Butyriboletus persolidus  (Schaeffer) D. Arora & J.L. Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 471. 2014;  Boletus appendiculatus  (Schaeff.) Secr. sensu Thiers  California Mushrooms: A Field Guide to the Boletes: 41. 1975
ENGLISH NAMES  butter bolete, oak bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) almost bald cap that varies from reddish brown to cinnamon brown to yellow-brown to yellowish or buff, 2) dense flesh with mild taste, 3) yellow pores that usually bruise blue quickly, and 4) finely netted yellow stem.^ RANGE This species has been confirmed only from CA where it occurs "in both the coastal region and in the Sierra Nevada foothills" (Arora(3)). It has been known as Boletus appendiculatus (Schaeff.) Secr. sensu Theirs. Arora(3) comment "Reports of Boletus appendiculatus sensu Thiers under conifers in the Pacific Northwest (e.g. Tylutki 1987) appear to be misidentifications based on Butyriboletus abieticola, Bu. primiregius and/or Bu. autumniregius. However, reports of Boletus appendiculatus with Q. garryana on Vancouver Island, British Columbia, are likely based on either this species and/or Bu. querciregius.", (Arora(3), with Latin names italicized). A collection from BC at the University of British Columbia as Boletus appendiculatus could be Butyriboletus persolidus or Butyriboletus querciregius.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh pale vinaceous with KOH, (Thiers, Bessette)
CAP  6-13cm, convex to spherical when young, becoming flat to flat-convex to shallowly depressed on disc, often highly irregular and uneven, margin incurved when young becoming broadly downcurved; buff to pale yellow when young, dull yellow to pale brown when older, often with stains or appearing overlaid with rust to red-colored blushes, occasionally with dark brown areas; dry to occasionally subviscid when wet, dull, bald to obscurely tomentose or velvety, sometimes shallowly cracked to areolate [cracked like dried mud] when old^, (Thiers), 6-20cm, cushion-shaped to convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old, margin incurved at first and typically with narrow band of sterile tissue; "reddish brown to rusty brown or yellow-brown, sometimes pale to dull yellow, when young", dry, bald or nearly so, (Bessette), 6-20(30)cm, smooth or with a fine bloom when young, (Arora(1))
FLESH  1-3cm thick, pale yellow to yellow, "unchanging or slowly changing to blue when exposed", in stem yellow near surface, whitish in center, unchanging or turning slightly blue in irregularly distributed areas when exposed^, (Thiers), firm and dense; "pale yellow to yellow, not staining or slowly and erratically blueing when exposed", (Bessette), flesh in base usually pinkish or vinaceous (Arora(1))
PORES  up to 1 mm broad, angular; yellow becoming dark yellow, turning blue when bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, deeply and broadly to narrowly depressed^, (Thiers), 1-3 per mm, angular; pale yellow becoming yellow to dark yellow or olive-yellow, turning blue when bruised; tube layer 0.8-2cm thick, depressed near stem when old, (Bessette), usually turning blue when bruised, but sometimes not (especially in button stage), (Arora(1))
STEM  6-9cm x 2-4cm, typically bulbous to wider in middle, sometimes equal, solid, occasionally with root-like prolongation; yellow, often with vinaceous to reddish brown stains, reticulation yellow or sometimes vinaceous to reddish; dry, reticulate [with a network] for two thirds of length^, (Thiers), 5-14cm x 2-5cm, bulbous or wider in middle or sometimes equal, solid; yellow with reddish brown stains especially near base; dry, with a fine inconspicuous reticulation [network] on upper part, (Bessette), 5-15cm x 2-6cm, (Arora(1))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Thiers, Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Thiers, Bessette)
EDIBILITY  yes, considered choice by some, but because of taxonomic confusion, use restraint, (Thiers)^, yes, considered choice by some, (Bessette), some people are "allergic" to it (Arora(1))
HABITAT  single to gregarious under hardwoods^, (Thiers), single, scattered, or in groups on ground under oak, September to January, (Bessette), fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark olive brown^ (Thiers), olive brown (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (10)12-14(16) x 4-5(6) microns, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped] to subcylindric [somewhat cylindric], smooth, individual spores brown in Melzer's, amyloid in mass, pale ochraceous in KOH, thin-walled^; basidia 4-spored, 26-31 x 9-12 microns, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia 48-65 x 8-13 microns, fusoid-ventricose, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle differentiated as a trichodermium of interwoven to tangled hyphae, pale brown in KOH, not incrusted; stem cuticle fertile on ridges, interwoven in other parts; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 12-16 x 4-6 microns, somewhat cylindric to somewhat fusoid, smooth, ochraceous, (Bessette), spores 12-15 x 3.5-5 microns (Arora(1))
NAME ORIGIN  from "per", Latin for "very" and "solidus", Latin for "solid" or "dense"
SIMILAR  Boletus regius was originally described as a variety but "is easily distinguished, however, by the beautiful old-rose color of the pileus, the often massive size of the basidiocarp, and the rather short, lemon-yellow stipe", (Thiers). Caloboletus marshii has a bitter taste, paler cap, and non-reticulate stem, (Arora(1)). See also SIMILAR section of Butyriboletus abieticola.
SOURCES  Thiers(1) (as Boletus appendiculatus), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus appendiculatus), Arora(1)* (as Boletus appendiculatus), Both(1) (as Boletus appendiculatus), AroraPocket* (as Boletus appendiculatus), Arora(3), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Butyriboletus primiregius  D. Arora & J.L. Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 474. 2014;  Boletus regius Krombh.  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) large size, 2) cap that is red or vinaceous red (the color often fading when exposed to sunlight), dry, and bald or breaking up into small scales, 3) flesh that is yellow but vinaceous tinged in the stem base, 4) small yellow pores that become greenish, and turn blue when bruised, 5) bulbous to equal stem that is yellow with dark red stains toward base, and yellow-reticulate in upper part, staining blue when bruised or rubbed, 6) fruiting under true fir and other conifers in the mountains in spring, and 7) spores with length to width ratio 3.5.^ The description is derived from Arora(3). RANGE Collections were examined from CA, OR, and ID.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  8-25(30)cm across, convex becoming broadly convex when mature or at times flat or undulating [wavy]; "at first wholly or mostly dark red or vinaceous red (but color often obscured by a layer of brown dust), often developing dingy brown, tan, ocher-brown or dingy olive brown tones as it ages or is exposed to light", "the vinaceous red tints sometimes disappearing entirely or more often remaining visible at least near the margin"; dry or slightly tacky but not viscid, bald or breaking up to form many scales (the scales much smaller than in Butyriboletus abieticola)
FLESH  firm, dense, thick; "pale yellow, typically unchanging in the button stage but at maturity usually turning blue at least somewhat when cut (often slowly and erratically and most pronounced just above the tube layer)"; in stem "firm, yellow except for the base, which usually has a distinct vinaceous tinge, not turning blue or turning blue only slightly when cut but larval tunnels often rhubarb red"
PORES  typically less than 1 millimeter wide, pore surface usually the same color as the tubes which are "pale yellow becoming bright yellow and eventually olive yellow or greenish", pore surface "typically turning blue quickly when bruised except in the button stage"; tubes at first short in relation to the cap flesh but 1-2cm or longer when mature
STEM  5-15(25)cm x 2-7cm wide at top, usually clavate [club-shaped] or bulbous when young but often more or less equal when old; "butter yellow to bright yellow throughout or commonly with dark red (rhubarb red) stains toward the base, often bruising blue when touched or rubbed"; finely reticulate over at least the upper part, "the reticulation yellow but often becoming brownish after spores are shed, rarely reddish"; base often ending in one or more root-like processes deep in the soil
ODOR  
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or in groups in soil under conifers (primarily Abies), fruiting in late spring and early summer (May to July), in the mountains, not known from the coastal region
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark olive brown, at least in mass under microscope
MICROSCOPIC  spores (12)13-17(18) x 3.5-5 microns, length to width ratio 3.5, fusoid, smooth, thin-walled, "pale brown or yellowish in KOH"^; basidia 4-spored, clavate; "hymenial cystidia fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle "an interwoven trichodermium with few if any erect hyphal tips"
NAME ORIGIN  from "primi" meaning "first" because it is the first butter bolete to fruit in the year in the western USA mountains, and "regius" meaning "kingly" and referring also to the European Butyriboletus regius
SIMILAR  Butyriboletus autumniregius fruits in fall, and the cap does not tend to fade as much (which could be due to environment) (Arora(3)). Butyriboletus querciregius 1) grows with oak, 2) has shorter spores and a smaller length to width ratio (3.0), and 3) has the cap color described differently (pinkish red, pinkish brown, or yellowish when fresh). The cap color of the cap of B. querciregius is also referred to as "red or dull red or even orange-red, sometimes blotched with yellow before fading to pinkish brown, tan or yellowish". (Arora(3)). Butyriboletus regius, known from Europe turns blue only rarely when cut (Arora(3)). Butyriboletus abieticola has fibrillose scales when mature (Arora(3)).
SOURCES  Arora(3)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Butyriboletus querciregius  D. Arora & J. L. Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 476. 2014;  Boletus regius Krombh.  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) large size, 2) cap that is reddish or dull red, often with yellow parts, dry, and bald or breaking up into small scales, 3) flesh that is yellow but vinaceous tinged in the stem base, 4) small yellow pores that become greenish, and turn blue when bruised, 5) bulbous to equal stem that is yellow with dark red to dark red blushes toward base, and yellow-reticulate in upper part, staining blue when bruised or rubbed, 6) fruiting under oak, usually in fall, and 7) spores with length to width ratio 3.0.^ The description is derived from Arora(3). RANGE Collections were examined from WA and CA, and its DNA sequence occurred on a Quercus garryana root tip in OR.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  6-20(25)cm across, convex becoming broadly convex when mature or sometimes flat; dry or slightly tacky but not viscid; "reddish or dull red when young and fresh or yellow overlain with red (i.e. reddish orange overall with more yellow at the margin) or red blotched with yellow but often fading to pale reddish brown, pinkish tan, yellow or yellowish buff with age or where exposed to light"; bald "or occasionally breaking up to form some small scales"
FLESH  "firm, thick"; "pale yellow, typically unchanging in the button stage" but when old "usually turning blue at least somewhat when cut"; in stem "firm, pale yellow except for the base, which usually has a distinct vinaceous tinge, turning blue little if at all when cut"
PORES  typically less than 1 millimeter wide, pore surface usually the same color as the tubes which are "pale yellow becoming bright yellow and eventually olive yellow or greenish", pore surface "typically turning blue quickly when bruised except in the button stage"; tubes at first short in relation to the cap flesh but 1-2cm or longer when mature
STEM  5-15(20)cm x 2-6cm at top, usually clavate [club-shaped] or slightly bulbous when young, often more or less equal when old; "butter yellow to bright yellow throughout or with beautiful red to dark red blushes toward the base, sometimes bruising blue when pressed but variable in this respect", finely reticulate over at least the upper part, "the reticulation yellow or occasionally reddish but often becoming brown after spores are shed"
ODOR  
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single or in groups in leaf litter under oak (mainly Quercus agrifolia and Q. garryana, less commonly with other Quercus spp.); mostly fruiting in fall (September to December) but has been found in May and in June
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark olive brown, at least in mass under microscope
MICROSCOPIC  spores (10)11-15(16) x 3.5-5 microns, length to width ratio 3.0, fusoid, smooth, thin-walled, "pale brown or yellowish in KOH"^; basidia 4-spored, clavate; "hymenial cystidia mostly fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle "an interwoven trichodermium with few erect hyphal tips"
NAME ORIGIN  from "querci" meaning "pertaining to oak" and "regius" meaning "kingly" and referring also to the European Butyriboletus regius
SIMILAR  Butyriboletus primiregius fruits in the spring and the cap color is described differently. Butyriboletus autumniregius 1) has not been found to form ectomycorrhizal associations with oak, 2) has a more vivid cap color "(tending toward deep rose red or vinaceous red when fresh rather than pinkish red, pinkish brown or yellowish)", and 3) has longer spores with a larger length to width ratio (3.5). Butyriboletus regius, known from Europe, turns blue only rarely when cut, (Arora(3)). Butyriboletus abieticola has fibrillose scales when mature and is associated with conifers, especially Abies (true fir), (Arora(3)).
SOURCES  Arora(3)*, Arora(1)* (photo as Boletus regius), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Butyriboletus regius  (Krombholz) D. Arora & J.L. Frank^  Mycologia 106(3): 466. 2013  group;  Boletus regius  Krombh.
ENGLISH NAMES  red-capped butter bolete, royal bolete
NOTES  Butyriboletus regius group is distinguished by its pink to reddish cap, yellow pores which in the Pacific Northwest stain blue, yellow flesh, and large thick reticulate stem, (Arora 1986).^ The true Butyriboletus regius is known from Europe. Its relatives in western North America have been redescribed as Butyriboletus querciregius, Butyriboletus primiregius, and Butyriboletus autumniregius (see separate entries). These species are separated (though not markedly) by molecular methods, and differ also in habitat, fruiting season, the extent of pinkish to vinaceous coloring, the presence of fibrillose scales on cap, and subtle differences in spore size. RANGE Members of this group are found at least in WA, OR, and CA (Arora(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap surface pale purplish with application of FeSO4, flesh grayish with application of FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  6-20cm or more, convex to flat or somewhat irregular; "pink to rosy-red or dark red, sometimes also with brownish tones or yellow areas"; "smooth to uneven or pitted, sometimes minutely hairy when young, dry or slightly viscid"^, (Arora(1)), 7-20cm, convex to nearly flat, margin incurved at first, with narrow band of tissue or sometimes even; "rose-pink to rose-red, sometimes with yellow or brown tones, especially near the margin"; dry, velvety-subtomentose at first, becoming bald at maturity, often pitted or wrinkled, especially when old, (Bessette), 8-20cm, convex to broadly convex when young, unchanging or becoming flat-convex to irregular in outline when old, typically very massive; bright pink to rose pink during all stages, occasionally with brown areas when young, and often becoming somewhat yellowish toward margin when old, or may fade to almost yellow with only pinkish spots or flushes; dry to moist, tomentose to velvety when young, unchanging or becoming bald when old, sometimes highly uneven and conspicuously pitted during all stages, (Thiers)
FLESH  very thick, firm; yellow, slowly and erratically turning blue when exposed, in base often pinkish or vinaceous^, (Arora(1)), yellow, typically turning blue slowly and erratically when exposed, (Bessette), 1.5-4.5cm thick, intense yellow, slowly and incompletely changing to blue when exposed; in stem "yellow in cortical region, changing to whitish or pallid in center", reddish to pale vinaceous at the base, unchanging when exposed, (Thiers)
PORES  bright yellow becoming darker yellow or olive yellow when old, usually turning blue when bruised, tubes yellow^, (Arora(1)), 1-2 per mm, angular; bright yellow becoming darker yellow, turning blue when bruised; tubes 0.8-2.5cm deep, depressed near stem, (Bessette), 0.5-1mm wide, angular, colored as tubes, turning blue when bruised; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick, adnexed when young, becoming deeply and broadly depressed when old, bright yellow when young, unchanging or becoming deeper yellow when old, turning blue when exposed, (Thiers)
STEM  4-14cm x 2.5-6cm, usually bulbous or thicker in lower part, often short in relation to cap, solid, "pale to bright yellow but usually bruising blue and often reddish-stained in lower part"; "finely reticulate over at least the upper half"^, (Arora(1)), 5-13.5cm x 2.5-5cm, widening downward and often bulbous, typically with ventricose base, solid; "bright yellow, often with reddish tints, especially near the base"; dry, fine yellow reticulation overall or at least on upper half; partial veil and ring absent, (Bessette), 7-11.5cm x 3-5cm at top, "usually conspicuously bulbous, sometimes clavate-bulbous or abruptly bulbous to ventricose", solid; bright yellow, "dry, finely but distinctly reticulate for two thirds of or entire length", reticulations colored as surface, (Thiers)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Thiers)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Thiers)
EDIBILITY  edible (Bessette)
HABITAT  single to gregarious in woods^ (Arora(1)), single, scattered or in groups under oaks or conifers, especially firs, August to November or sometimes May to June, (Bessette), spring, summer, fall, winter
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^, (Bessette), pale olive-brown to olive-brown (Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12.5-16.5 x 3.5-5 microns, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora(1)), spores 12-17 x 4-5 microns, elliptic to fusoid [spindle-shaped], smooth, colorless to pale ochraceous, (Bessette), spores 12.5-16.5 x 3.7-5 microns, elliptic to fusoid to subcylindric, smooth, ochraceous in Melzer's reagent, pale ochraceous to colorless in KOH, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 27-32 x 7-9 microns, clavate, colorless; hymenial cystidia scattered to numerous, (more abundant on pores), 25-45 x 8-17 microns, clavate to fusoid to subcylindric, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a trichodermium of tangled to somewhat repent hyphae with free, more or less erect hyphal tips, appearing amyloid"; stem cuticle a layer of fertile basidia and hymeniform to vesiculose cells with interspersed caulocystidia similar to the hymenial cystidia; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means "regal, magnificent"^ (Both)
SIMILAR  Boletus abieticola has fibrillose scales at maturity (Arora(3)). Butyriboletus appendiculatus has a reddish brown to rusty brown or a yellow brown cap (Bessette). In the San Francisco Bay area, "there seems to be considerable intergradation" between Butyriboletus appendiculatus and Butyriboletus regius, (Thiers).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus regius), Thiers(1) (as Boletus regius), Arora(1)* (as Boletus regius), Courtecuisse(1)* (as Boletus regius), Both(1) (as Boletus regius), Buczacki(1)* (as Boletus regius), Arora(3)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Caloboletus 'calopus'  (Pers.) Vizzini^  Index Fungorum 146: 1. 2014;  Boletus calopus  Fr. 
ENGLISH NAMES  bitter bolete
NOTES  The Pacific Northwest status of boletes resembling Caloboletus calopus is not clear. Features of Caloboletus calopus include brown cap, yellow pores, a reticulated stem that is yellow to red, flesh and pores that turn rapidly blue when damaged, and bitter taste. Caloboletus frustosus [also known as Boletus calopus var. frustosus and as Boletus frustosus Snell & E.A. Dick and Caloboletus frustosus (Snell & E.A. Dick) D. Arora & J.L. Frank] is said by Bessette(3) to have "a conspicuously rimose-areolate to fissured yellowish brown to grayish brown pileus at maturity" which Boletus calopus var. calopus is "almost identical but has a nearly smooth pileus that does not become conspicuously rimose-areolate to fissured at maturity". The Bessette(3) description of the cap context says "unchanging when exposed" for var. frustosus and "rapidly staining when cut" for var. calopus, and the spore measurements given are different as well. But according to Siegel(2), based on preliminary genetic analysis, it is possible that Caloboletus frustosus is the same species as Caloboletus conifericola - if so the name Caloboletus frustosus would take precedence.^ RANGE Caloboletus calopus var. calopus is said to be fairly common in the Pacific Northwest, and also occurs in MI, NH, and NY, (Bessette as Boletus calopus var. calopus). There are collections at the University of Washington labeled as Boletus calopus (variety not specified) from Washington and Oregon, and also to occur at the University of British Columbia from British Columbia. Caloboletus frustosus is said to be fairly common from the Rocky Mountains of Idaho west to Washington and south to California, (Bessette as Boletus calopus var. frustosus). There is also a 1942 collection from Oregon as Boletus frustosus at the University of Washington.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains yellow-brown with application of KOH and olive-green with FeSO4 (Bessette)
CAP  6-20cm, obtuse becoming convex to broadly convex, margin incurved at first, often with narrow band of sterile tissue; "olive-brown to gray-brown at first, becoming paler olive-brown to dark yellow-brown in age"; dry, somewhat tomentose when young, becoming nearly smooth when mature, usually rimose-areolate [cracked like dried mud] over disc when old^, (Bessette), 10-30cm, convex to flat or irregular; dull brown to olive-brown, grayish brown, or olive-buff, sometimes yellow-brown or when old darker brown; "dry, dull, smooth to minutely hairy or fibrillose", often areolate [cracked like dried mud] when old, (Arora), dull buffy tan to olive-brown or yellow-brown, (Phillips)
FLESH  "whitish to pale yellow, rapidly staining blue when cut"^, (Bessette), very thick; "pale yellow or whitish, quickly blueing when exposed", (Arora)
PORES  1-2 per mm, round, becoming angular when mature; pale yellow at first, becoming olive-yellow when mature, rapidly turning blue when bruised; tubes up to 2cm long, often depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), pale yellow becoming darker or dingier yellow when old, typically bruising blue or blue-green quickly; tubes similar, (Arora)
STEM  7-15cm x 2-5cm, nearly equal or widening downward, solid; "carmine-red to nearly scarlet, typically dark brownish toward the base and yellowish toward the apex", dry, conspicuously reticulate [netted] at least over upper part, the reticulation whitish to pinkish^, (Bessette), 6-15(20)cm x 3-7cm, equal or bulbous, solid, firm; "yellow, but usually with pink to red zones or discolorations also present, or sometimes entirely reddish", turning blue when bruised; top or upper half finely reticulate, (Arora), yellow to slightly orange-yellow, reticulum whitish, base pinkish, (Phillips)
ODOR  somewhat pungent or not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  bitter^ (Bessette), distinctly and persistently bitter (Arora)
EDIBILITY  unknown, bitter, (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups on ground in conifer or mixed forests, sometimes with oak^, (Bessette), "solitary, scattered, or in groups on ground in mixed woods and under conifers in the late summer and fall (or occasionally spring)", (Arora)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive brown^ (Bessette), dark olive-brown (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-19 x 5-6 microns, elliptic, smooth, yellowish^; conspicuous amyloid septa are found in cap trama and hymenophoral trama, (Bessette), spores 13-19 x 4-6 microns, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth, (Arora), spores 10.8-15.6 x 3.7-5.1 microns, fusiform-elliptic, smooth, yellowish, thick-walled, with droplets; basidia 4-spored, 33-48 x 10-13 microns, clavate, without basal clamp connection; cheilocystidia 35-50 x 7-12 microns, fusiform, pleurocystidia 50-80 x 8-10 microns, fusiform; cap cuticle a trichoderm of intertwined hyphae, some erect and exserted, 3-7 microns wide, with brownish pigmentation, septa without clamp connections; caulocystidia on the center of the stem 50-90 x 18-27 microns, fusiform to rostrate, thick-walled, (Breitenbach), spores 14-17 x 4-6 microns, elliptic to subfusoid, smooth, inamyloid, colorless to pale yellow in KOH, moderately thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 30-45 x 10-13 microns, clavate; hymenial cystidia scattered, inconspicuous, 33-48 x 5-10 microns, narrowly fusoid-ventricose to cylindric, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a loosely interwoven trichodermium staining ochraceous in KOH with numerous free hyphal tips, often showing incrustations, hyphae 6-10 microns wide"; stem cuticle a layer of basidia and cystidia similar to hymenium, (Thiers for var. frustosus)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'beautiful stem'
SIMILAR  Other large bitter boletes are Caloboletus conifericola, with yellow stem (having no red) and darker (olive-gray to deep brown) cap, and Caloboletus rubripes with a non-reticulate stem, (Arora). Caloboletus marshii has a non-reticulate stem and lacks conspicuous red on the stem (Arora). See also SIMILAR section of Boletus smithii and Caloboletus conifericola.
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus calopus), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus calopus), Arora(1) (as Boletus calopus), Courtecuisse(1)* (as Boletus calopus), Thiers(1) (as Boletus calopus var. frustosus), Breitenbach(3)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Caloboletus conifericola  Vizzini^  Index Fungorum 146: 1. 2014;  Boletus coniferarum  E.A. Dick and Snell
ENGLISH NAMES  conifer Boletus
NOTES  Features include 1) dark olive-gray to gray-brown cap, 2) white to pale yellow flesh that turns instantly blue when bruised, 3) yellow stem with olive-brown to blackish tints near base, 4) fine reticulation [network pattern] on upper stem, and 5) bitter taste.^ The description is derived from Bessette except where indicated. According to Siegel(2), based on preliminary genetic analysis, it is possible that Caloboletus frustosus (with salmon to red lower stem) is the same species as Caloboletus conifericola - if so the name Caloboletus frustosus would take precedence (see Caloboletus calopus for further details). RANGE Caloboletus conifericola is found in BC, WA, OR, and AK, (Bessette). It has been reported from ID (A. Parker, pers. comm., K. Chadwick, pers. comm.). Caloboletus conifericola is found rarely in Humboldt and Del Norte counties in CA (Desjardin(6)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  7.5-30cm, margin incurved at first; dark olive-gray becoming grayish brown or dark brown, sometimes streaked reddish or blackish; subtomentose to bald, often cracked or cracked-areolate when old^, (Bessette), up to 15cm, milky coffee color or "grayish brown to dark brown, at times with slight tinges of red and black, where handled the lighter colors becoming darker brown to blackish"; "outer portions finely diffract", (Dick), surface unpolished to somewhat velvety (Trudell)
FLESH  white to pale yellow, becoming instantly blue when exposed^, (Bessette), "white to pale yellow, with some areas of pinkish or pale red, instantly changing to clear blue"; in stem white or yellow in upper part, more yellow downward, basal part deep reddish brown, changing instantly to clear blue, (Dick)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round to angular; "pale yellow to olivaceous at first, becoming dingy yellow at maturity, instantly blueing when bruised"; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick^, (Bessette), minute; pale yellow, lemon-yellow to olivaceous, later dingy yellow or dingy buff, instantly changing to clear blue and eventually to brown when bruised; tube layer depressed-subdecurrent, the same color except change to brown not mentioned, (Dick)
STEM  5-12cm x 2-5cm, widening downward or sometimes equal; "pale yellow to yellow with olive-brown to blackish tints near the base, lacking reddish coloration, rapidly blueing when bruised"; finely reticulate [with netted pattern] on upper part or nearly overall, "or sometimes very obscurely reticulate"^, (Bessette), 11cm long and 4cm wide at top, more or less equal or somewhat bulbous, widest parts up to 5.5cm wide; "pale yellow to yellow, with tinges of reddish brown in scattered areas, the very base olivaceous-brown or dark brown to black, changing everywhere to blue where handled"; subglabrous [nearly bald] or minutely furfuraceous, "finely double-reticulate to the base", (Dick), 10-15cm x 3-6cm (Phillips)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Thiers), pleasant (Phillips)
TASTE  bitter^ (Bessette, Dick)
EDIBILITY  no, because bitter, (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups under conifers^, (Bessette)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12-16.5 x 4-5.5(6) microns, elliptic to oblong-elliptic, pale yellowish green in 2% KOH^; cheilocystidia and pleurocystidia scarce, 27-47 x 7-10 microns, "ventricose to ventricose-rostrate with short necks thin or thick, some necks bent or twisted", colorless, a few pale yellow; hyphae of cap surface, flesh, and trama "staining very pale gray to gray in Melzer's solution, the crosswalls with a distinct peripheral amyloid ring", (Dick), spores 12-17 x 4-6 microns, elliptic to oblong-elliptic, smooth, yellowish, (Bessette)
NAME ORIGIN  means "occurring with conifers"^ (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Caloboletus calopus has an olive-brown to brown cap and stem "with carmine-red that does not stain blue when bruised", (Bessette). C. calopus is more reticulate (Phillips). Caloboletus frustosus has conspicuously cracked cap surface when mature and pink to reddish color on stem, (Trudell(4) as Boletus calopus var. frustosus).
SOURCES  Dick(1) (as Boletus coniferarum), Bessette(3)* (as Boletus coniferarum), Trudell(4)* (as Boletus coniferarum), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus coniferarum), Ammirati(1)* (as Boletus coniferarum), Thiers(1) (as Boletus coniferarum), Both(1) (as Boletus coniferarum), Desjardin(6) (as Boletus coniferarum), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Caloboletus marshii  D. Arora, C.F. Schwarz & J.L. Frank^  Index Fungorum 194: 1. 2014
ENGLISH NAMES  Ben's bitter bolete
NOTES  Features of Caloboletus marshii include 1) dry to slightly viscid, whitish dry cap that becomes buff or tan when old, the surface dry to slightly viscid, faintly velvety and with a whitish bloom when young, 2) yellow pores and tubes, 3) blue injury reaction of flesh, tubes, pores, and stem, 4) yellow, bulbous, non-reticulate stem, 5) bitter taste, and 6) occurrence with oaks.^ RANGE Caloboletus marshii is known from CA to OR and WA (Frank).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  6-15(20)cm across, convex becoming broadly convex, "margin often projecting slightly beyond the tube layer (curblike) when young"; white to grayish white becoming buff or tan when old; "dry or very slightly viscid when wet, faintly velvety and with a fine whitish, powdery bloom when young"^, (Frank), 5-25cm across, convex to nearly flat or somewhat irregular; "whitish to gray, pale grayish-brown or buff when young, becoming dull brownish" and developing darker brown stains when old or upon handling; dry, smooth or cracking when old, (Arora)
FLESH  "pallid and readily bruising blue or gray-blue when fresh" but often only weakly so when old; stem flesh "often marked with zones of contrasting colors", upper yellow parts colored as cap flesh and usually turning blue when cut, dull yellowish to dingy brown or even reddish brown at or below the mid-point and turning blue less strongly or not at all; extreme base usually covered in a thin or thick (0.05-0.3cm) yellow rind of tissue^, (Frank), "thick, dense"; "white or grayish, turning blue to bluish-gray erratically when exposed", (Arora)
PORES  pores less than 0.1cm in diameter, concolorous with tube layer, "staining blue when fresh, the blue stains becoming deep grayish-blue and finally changing to brown"; tube layer 1-3cm deep, "pale yellow becoming yellow and then olive-yellow", or when old dingy olive-gray^, (Frank), pores and tubes "pale yellow becoming dingy yellow or olive-yellow, bruising blue quickly, then eventually turning dingy brown", (Arora)
STEM  5-15cm x 2-7cm, "narrowest at the apex and swollen or bulbous below with the base often narrowed and/or knobby and radicating, typically terminating in a knotted tuft of yellow rhizomorphs"; stem surface "pale yellow to yellow, sometimes with small reddish dots or splotches (especially where damaged by invertebrates), and often stained dingy brownish toward base" (more so when old), yellow parts typically turning blue when handled; stem not reticulate^, (Frank), 4-15cm x 2-5cm at top, usually enlarged in lower part but sometimes tapered at the base; pallid to buff or yellow in upper part, "sometimes with a very slight reddish zone", dingy brown in lower part, darker brown throughout when old; turning blue when bruised; smooth, not reticulate, (Arora)
ODOR  "rather distinctive, somewhat sour"^ (Frank)
TASTE  "distinctly bitter in fresh specimens at least for most people, but sometimes indistinctly in old or waterlogged specimens"^ (Frank), "bitter, at least latently", (Arora), slightly sweet at first then slowly somewhat bitter (the sweetness cutting the bitter sensation), (M. Beug, pers. comm.)
EDIBILITY  inedible (Arora)
HABITAT  single "or in groups or small clusters on ground in association with oaks", especially Quercus agrifolia (California Live Oak) but not limited to that species, also with Quercus garryana (Oregon White Oak)^, (Frank), single to gregarious on ground near or under live oak in summer and early fall (Arora)
SPORE DEPOSIT  "dark olive-brown in mass"^ (Frank), olive-brown (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-14 x 4.5-6 microns, short fusoid or long elliptic "with a weak abaxial curve", "smooth, pale brown or yellowish in KOH"^; basidia clavate, mostly 4-spored; "hymenial cystidia mostly fusoid-ventricose"; cap cuticle "an entangled upright layer of sinuous, cylindrical hyphae", (Frank), spores 11-14 x 4.5-6 microns, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth, (Arora)
NAME ORIGIN  "Named after Ben Marsh, who found it near the type locality in 1972" and brought it to the attention of David Arora "after unsuccessfully trying to make it palatable"^ (Frank)
SIMILAR  Caloboletus calopus has a reticulate stem (Arora). Caloboletus rubripes lacks the pale cap and has red color on the stem (Arora). C. rubripes shows red on the stem and associates with conifers, (Frank). Butyriboletus persolidus (as Boletus appendiculatus) lacks the pale cap color and the bitter taste, and has a reticulate stem, (Arora). B. persolidus has a brown cap, mild taste, yellow reticulate stem, and yellow flesh that turns blue erratically, (Frank). Boletus barrowsii lacks the bitter taste, has a reticulate stem, and has white pores when young (Arora). B. barrowsii has a sweet or nutty taste, reticulate stem, and non-blueing flesh, (Frank).
SOURCES  Frank(5), Arora(1)* (as Boletus "marshii"), Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Caloboletus rubripes  (Thiers) Vizzini^  Index Fungorum 146: 1. 2014;  Boletus rubripes  Thiers;  Caloboletus calopus  (Pers.) Vizzini  (misapplied name)
ENGLISH NAMES  bitter bolete, red-stemmed bitter bolete
NOTES  Features include a pale buff to tan cap, yellow flesh and pores that turn blue on exposure, a smooth stem that when young is yellow at the top and red at the base but when old is typically dark red throughout, and a usually bitter taste.^ RANGE Caloboletus rubripes is found in the Pacific Northwest, Southwest, and Mexico, and is especially common in NM and CO, (Bessette). It is specifically found in WA and OR (collections at the University of Washington), and is recorded also from ID and CA, (Michelle Seidl, pers. comm.). It has been reported from MT (Larry Evans, pers. comm.), A Paul Kroeger collection from BC is deposited at University of British Columbia.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains yellow-orange with application of KOH (Bessette)
CAP  6-15(25)cm, convex, sometimes becoming flat; "pale buff to olive-buff to tan"; dry, dull, smooth or velvety, often cracked or furrowed when old^, (Arora), 4-18cm, convex to cushion-shaped, expanding, margin incurved to inrolled for a long time, even; "buff to olive-buff to olive-brown, staining brown when bruised"; "dry, velvety-tomentose to appressed-fibrillose", often cracked-areolate when old, (Bessette), pale buff to dull tawny brown, darker where handled (Phillips), when young pale buff, unchanging or becoming pale brown when old, sometimes fading to buff on the margin, typically staining brown when bruised or handled, (Thiers)
FLESH  thick, firm; "buff to pale yellow or even whitish", but turning blue when exposed^, (Arora), whitish to pale yellow, instantly turning blue when exposed, (Bessette), up to 3cm thick, buff to yellow, turning blue immediately on exposure; in stem yellow, dark red to almost black at base, turning blue when exposed, (Thiers), cream to yellow or rarely pinkish in cap, white to yellow in upper stem, and red to vinaceous in base; when cut, blueing instantly, (Trudell)
PORES  pale yellow becoming darker or duller yellow when old, turning blue when bruised^, (Arora), 1-3 per mm, angular, "yellow at first, becoming olive-yellow at maturity", instantly turning blue when bruised; tube layer 0.8-1.6cm thick, (Bessette), tube layer shallowly depressed when young, deeply depressed when old, (Thiers)
STEM  7-13(20)cm x 1-4cm, sometimes swollen in lower part (especially when young), firm, solid; usually yellow at top with bright pink to reddish areas in lower part, becoming dark red throughout when old; not reticulate but often longitudinally striate when old^, (Arora), 5-12cm x 2-4.5cm, nearly equal or widening or narrowing downward, solid; yellow near top and pinkish red to purple-red overall when old, quickly turning blue, then slowly staining grayish olive when bruised, mycelium whitish to pale yellow; dry, bald or sometimes longitudinally striate, lacking reticulation; partial veil and ring absent, (Bessette), yellow mycelium at base (Thiers)
ODOR  unpleasant or not distinctive (Bessette), often strong, unpleasant (Thiers)
TASTE  usually bitter^ (Arora), bitter (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  inedible because bitter (Bessette)
HABITAT  single to scattered or grouped primarily under conifers^ (Arora), single, scattered or in groups under conifers or sometimes oak, July to November, (Bessette), in Pacific Northwest in montane conifer forests; in California described from coastal conifer and mixed forests, (Trudell), summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Arora, Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 12.5-17.5 x 4-5 microns, spindle-shaped to elliptic, smooth^, (Arora), spores 12-18 x 4-5 microns, subfusoid to subcylindric, smooth, ochraceous, (Bessette), spores 12.5-17.6 x 4-5 microns, subcylindric to subfusoid to narrowly subelliptic, smooth, ochraceous in KOH, dark ochraceous in Melzer's, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 26-30 x 7-13 microns; hymenial cystidia scattered, 24-35 x 5-7 microns, embedded in hymenium, fusoid to occasionally clavate or fusoid-ventricose, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle a trichodermium of interwoven hyphae, occasionally incrusted; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means "red stem"^ (Both)
SIMILAR  Caloboletus calopus has a reticulated stem and paler cap, and does not usually have a completely dark red stem when old, (Thiers). Caloboletus marshii has a paler cap and lacks conspicuous red on the stem, and associates with oak, (Arora). See also SIMILAR section of Boletus smithii.
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus), Arora(1)* (as Boletus), Thiers(1) (as Boletus), Trudell(4)* (as Boletus), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus), Both(1) (as Boletus), AroraPocket* (as Boletus), Desjardin(6)* (as Boletus), Siegel(2)*
26
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Chalciporus piperatoides  (A.H. Sm. & Thiers)  T.J. Baroni and Both^  Mycologia 83: 559-564. 1991;  Boletus piperatoides  A.H. Sm. and Thiers
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a buff to yellow brown or rusty brown cap, 2) brownish flesh that typically turns blue when cut, 3) irregular somewhat radial pores that are yellow to brown and stain blue to bluish black when bruised, 4) a stem that is cap-colored except at the base where it is bright yellow, and 5) peppery taste.^ RANGE Chalciporus piperatoides is found from NY to CA (Bessette), in the Pacific Northwest at least in WA and OR, (collections at the University of Washington, observed in OR by L. Norvell, pers. comm.). It appears on foray lists from BC where it is much less common than Chalciporus piperatus.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle dark rusty brown to blackish with application of KOH, flesh dull brown to purplish red or gray with application of KOH, (Bessette), flesh bluish gray with FeSO4, with flesh in KOH a yellow pigment dissolves into the mount, the reaction of the cap surface soon brownish, (Smith)
CAP  3-8cm, obtuse to convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat, sometimes with broad umbo, margin may be uplifted when old; "buff to yellow-brown, orangish-brown or rusty brown"; smooth or nearly smooth, dry, viscid when moist; margin with narrow band of sterile tissue^, (Bessette), 3-6cm, obtuse becoming flat or the margin becoming uplifted; tan to dull orange-cinnamon, margin close to cinnamon-buff; soft (like kid leather), subviscid, (Smith)
FLESH  "pale pinkish buff to dull pinkish brown or dull yellow-brown", typically turning blue when cut, at least above the tubes^, (Bessette), thick, pale pinkish buff, when cut slowly dull blue; in stem base lemon-yellow, (Smith)
PORES  up to 1.5mm wide, irregular, somewhat boletinoid [radially arranged and elongated], "dull yellow at first, becoming yellow-brown to orangish brown, dull cinnamon or brown, often with an olive tinge, staining blue to bluish black when bruised"; tube layer 0.5-1cm thick^, (Bessette), 2-3 per mm; yellow-brown, staining bluish; tube layer up to 1cm thick, decurrent, dull ochraceous, staining dingy inky blue when bruised, (Smith) 
STEM  3-8cm x 0.5-1.6cm, nearly equal, solid; colored as cap or paler, with bright yellow basal mycelium; "dry, fibrillose-punctate to fibrous-striate"; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 4-6cm x 0.4-0.6cm, solid; dingy yellowish brown, base coated with lemon-yellow mycelium; bald apart from mycelium at base, (Smith)
ODOR  
TASTE  slowly acrid [peppery]^ (Bessette), slowly peppery (not as hot as B. piperatus), (Smith)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on ground under conifers or hardwoods^, (Bessette), fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark smoky olive when fresh, rusty cinnamon when dry^, (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6-10 x 3-4 microns, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, yellowish to pale brown^, (Bessette), spores 7-9(10) x 3-3.5 microns, somewhat boat-shaped, smooth, in Melzer's fleeting amyloid fresh and as revived pale bister to dingy pale tan, in KOH pale clay color soon fading to bright yellow singly and more brownish in groups; basidia 4-spored, 23-28 x 5.5-7.5 microns, clavate, colorless to yellowish in KOH; pleurocystidia 36-57 x 7-12 microns, narrowly fusoid-ventricose to subcylindric, colorless to yellow in KOH and Melzer's reagent, cheilocystidia similar but smaller and content yellow to yellow-brown; cap cuticle of "interwoven hyphae 4-8 microns wide, yellowish in KOH and with end-cells tubular to narrowly clavate, the walls showing a slight tendency to become roughened"; stem cuticle with fertile patches near top of stem with basidia and cystidia resembling those in hymenium; tube trama sections mounted in Melzer's and crushed become dark greenish to bluish green under microscope (but no localized amyloid reaction is present); clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'like [Chalciporus] piperatus'
SIMILAR  Chalciporus piperatus 1) does not turn blue, 2) its taste is more rapidly and strongly peppery, 3) its flesh is somewhat brighter in color, 4) the fresh spores lack a fleeting amyloid reaction, and 5) revived tube trama lacks a fleeting amyloid reaction, (Smith). C. piperatus has tubes not staining bluish and spore deposit brown rather than olive, (Trudell).
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Trudell(4)
27
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Chalciporus piperatus  (Bull.) Bataille^  Bolets p.19. 1908;  Boletus piperatus  Bull.
ENGLISH NAMES  peppery bolete, pepper bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) small yellowish brown to reddish brown cap, 2) yellowish flesh that does not turn blue, 3) angular pores that are cinnamon becoming reddish brown and not turning blue, 4) rather slender stem colored like cap except bright yellow at base, and 5) peppery taste.^ Some evidence suggests it is a mycoparasite on mycelium of Amanita muscaria (Desjardin(6)). Chalciporus piperatus is common in the Pacific Northwest, especially in the northern part. RANGE It is fairly common and widely distributed through North America, (Bessette). There are collections from WA, OR, AK and ID at the University of Washington and from BC at the University of British Columbia. Material was studied from CA (Thiers), and reported from AB (Schalkwijk-Barendsen), as well as Europe and Asia (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap stains dark reddish brown with application of KOH, and pale grayish green with FeSO4, flesh stains violet-gray with application of KOH and pale grayish green with FeSO4, (Bessette), with FeSO4 the line above the tubes is grayish, in KOH no reaction but on the cap surface staining dark red-brown, (Smith)
CAP  1.6-9cm, convex becoming nearly flat when old; "buff to yellow-brown, orange-brown or reddish brown"; bald or slightly fibrillose, dry or somewhat viscid, sometimes cracked when old^, (Bessette), 3-7cm, convex becoming flat-convex to flat or occasionally subumbonate [somewhat umbonate] when old; reddish brown to brown when young, when old fading to pale reddish brown; moist to dry, often subviscid when old or when wet, dull, bald to obscurely appressed-fibrillose, when old sometimes appearing obscurely rimose or slightly fibrillose-scaly, (Thiers), (1.5)2-5(9)cm, obtuse to convex, becoming flat to flat-convex to occasionally subumbonate or margin slightly turned up; clay color to yellow-brown or orange-cinnamon, often darker on disc to rusty cinnamon; dry becoming subviscid, mostly unpolished, bald to somewhat fibrillose-streaked or appressed-squamulose, always bald when old, at times areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Smith), dull when dry, somewhat lubricous when wet, (Breitenbach), 2-8cm but usually less than 5cm, (Arora), reddish brown to rust brown or vinaceous brown, often with a mix of yellowish brown, and sometimes becoming more ochraceous brown when old, (Trudell)
FLESH  "pale yellow or tinged reddish", becoming dingy purplish brown when old, not turning blue when cut or bruised; in stem lemon-yellow, not turning blue^, (Bessette), 0.5-1.5cm thick, yellow, changing to pale vinaceous to pink near the cap surface, "unchanging or darkly pigmented areas changing to grayish vinaceous when exposed", not turning blue; in stem yellow, unchanging or darkening slightly when exposed, (Thiers), up to 1cm thick, firm; pale yellow, when old often with slight rose tint or distinctly vinaceous above tubes, becoming dingy-vinaceous-buff when old; in stem lemon-yellow and unchanging, (Smith), in stem brownish buff in upper part and bright yellow in base (Trudell), in cap 0.5-1.5cm thick, "pale yellow with a vinaceous line below the cap cuticle, darkening to cinnamon-brown when bruised, not bluing" (Desjardin)
PORES  0.5-2mm wide, angular, sometimes radiating; "dull cinnamon, reddish cinnamon, or cinnamon-brown", becoming darker reddish brown when old, not turning blue when cut or bruised but sometimes staining brown; tube layer 0.3-1cm thick,^ (Bessette), 1-2mm, angular; red to reddish brown; tube layer 0.5-1cm thick, adnate to arcuate-decurrent when young, becoming depressed around stem when old, yellow to reddish yellow, unchanging or darkening slightly when bruised, (Thiers), 2 per mm average, angular, unequal, thin-walled; dull yellow when young, soon cinnamon and when old red to brick-red, not staining appreciably when injured (merely dark brown), tube layer 0.3-1cm thick, adnate to subdecurrent or faintly depressed, dingy ochraceous but slowly reddish when old, (Smith)
STEM  4-9.5cm x 0.6-1.2cm, equal or narrowing downward, solid; colored as cap or paler, base with bright yellow mycelium; partial veil and annulus absent,^ (Bessette), 2-4cm x 0.5-1cm at top, equal or narrowing downward, solid; reddish brown, usually more or less colored as cap, with copious bright yellow mycelium at base; dry, bald to slightly appressed-fibrillose, (Thiers), (2)4-10(12)cm x (0.3)0.4-1(1.5)cm, solid, reddish cinnamon from pruina, with yellow ground color, base bright yellow from mycelium, (Smith), more or less colored as cap or more yellowish in upper part (Courtecuisse)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette, Smith)
TASTE  distinctly hot and peppery^ (Bessette), very sharply and distinctly acrid [peppery] (Smith)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)^, McIlvaine reports that peppery taste is lost in cooking and then edible, but Murrill warns that it is poisonous, (Thiers)
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on ground under conifers or hardwoods,^ (Bessette), summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown to cinnamon brown^ (Bessette), cinnamon brown (Thiers, Desjardin), dull cinnamon (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 9-12 x 4-5 microns, narrowly fusiform, smooth, pale brown^, (Bessette), spores 8.5-12 x 3-4 microns, subfusoid to subelliptic, colorless to pale ochraceous in KOH, rust brown in Melzer's reagent; basidia 4-spored, 24-29 x 8-10 microns, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia abundant, conspicuous, 45-70 x 10-15 microns, "fusoid-ventricose to subclavate to fusoid with obtuse apices", colorless; cap cuticle a tangled trichodermium staining dark brown in KOH and rust brown in Melzer's reagent; stem cuticle a layer of fertile basidia and caulocystidia similar to the hymenium, with occasional areas lacking these and appearing as a layer of interwoven hyphae; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores (8.5)9-12 x 4-5 microns, narrowly fusiform, olivaceous-colorless to dingy ochraceous in KOH, rusty brown in Melzer's reagent; cap cuticle a tangled trichodermium of broad (10-17 microns) hyphae with fusoid to elliptic or cylindric end-cells; tube trama inamyloid, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means "peppery"^ (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Chalciporus piperatoides has 1) tubes and pores that turn blue when cut or injured, 2) taste that is slowly only somewhat peppery, 3) cap flesh that is dull-colored, 4) fresh spores with a fleeting amyloid reaction, and 5) revived tube trama with a fleeting amyloid reaction, (Smith). C. piperatoides has tubes staining bluish and spore deposit olive rather than brown (Trudell). C. piperatoides "turns blue where injured (best seen by bruising the pores) and has a pinkish cinnamon cap context, without a vinaceous line below the cuticle, and dark smoky olive spore print."
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)* (as Boletus piperatus), Lincoff(2)* (as Boletus piperatus), McKnight(1)* (as Boletus), Trudell(4)*, Miller(14)*, Breitenbach(3)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)* (as Boletus), Ammirati(1)* (as Boletus), Sept(1)* (as Boletus), Grund(11) (as Boletus), Thiers(1) (as Boletus), Smith(35), Both(1), Buczacki(1)*, Desjardin(6)*, Siegel(2)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gastroboletus ruber  (Zeller) Cazares and Trappe^  Mycotaxon 42: 339-345. 1991;  Truncocolumella rubra  Zeller
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Also listed in Truffles etc. category. Features include 1) nearly spherical to top-shaped or lobed fruitbody lacking a distinct cap, 2) rose to reddish brown covering that is smooth and thin, usually disappearing over the tube mouths which are tinged reddish orange to red, 3) tubes pale yellow to dark olive, and blue when bruised or exposed, not vertical, divided into narrow maze-like cavities, 4) short pale yellow stem-columella that branches into the interior of the spore mass and that turns blue when bruised or exposed, 5) development under the soil surface associated with mountain conifers, and 6) large spores.^ Gastroboletus ruber is the current name in the online Species Fungorum, accessed April 1, 2018, but in MycoBank, accessed the same day, the current name is Truncocolumella rubra. The species will likely be recombined into a bolete genus such as Neoboletus. Gastroboletus ruber is common among false truffles in the Pacific Northwest (Trappe(13)). RANGE Gastroboletus ruber is found in WA, OR, and CA, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  2-4cm x 2-5.5cm, nearly spherical to top-shaped or lobed, peridium [outer layer] rose to brown-red or red-brown and persistent at top of stem, dingy pale yellow to dark red-brown and usually disappearing where covering tube mouths but sometimes partially persisting and then becoming yellow-brown to cinnamon and depressed in the tube mouths to give a reticulate appearance^, (Cazares), 2-5cm broad, 2-4cm high, broadly pear-shaped to nearly spherical; peridium (outer layer) yellowish at first, becoming reddish where exposed, aniline yellow where covered by soil, surface thin and almost entirely disappearing by maturity, (Smith), 2-3.5cm broad, 2.5-3cm high, broadly pear-shaped, peridium thin, almost entirely disappearing by maturity, reddish, (Zeller)
FLESH  in stem yellowish white, turning blue when bruised or exposed^, (Zeller), in stem-columella pale yellow, colored as surface, turning instantly blue when cut, (Cazares)
PORES  initially of separable tubes 0.5-1.0 mm in diameter and divided into maze-like locules +/- 0.2 mm wide, on expansion, tubular organization disrupted but locules still radiate and are up to 0.5(0.8)mm wide; pale yellow when young, dark olive when mature, tube mouths tinged red-orange to red when mature, turning blue where bruised or cut^, (Cazares), cavities relatively large, maze-like; "dingy pale yellow, changing to bluish on bruising and exposure, drying raw umber to rusty brown", (Smith), cavities in spore mass relatively large, maze-like, spore mass sordid light yellow, turning blue on bruising or exposure, (Zeller), pores up to 1mm wide, angular to irregular, (Bessette)
STEM  up to 1.5cm broad at top when percurrent, columnar to dendroid, percurrent or not, with many branches reaching or nearly reaching the peridium, projecting as much as 1cm below the glebal base; pale yellow, turning blue instantly when cut^, (Cazares), 0.7-1.5cm thick, "almost percurrent at times and also with narrow plate-like branches radiating laterally and terminally, extended below as a sterile base", "yellowish, changing to blue when bruised or exposed, drying yellowish", (Smith), columella stout, 0.7-1.2cm broad, "almost percurrent, with narrow branches radiating laterally and terminally", "extended below as a sterile base", (Zeller), 1-2cm x 1-2cm, tapered downward; colored like cap; dry, (Bessette)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Cazares)
TASTE  
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  fruitbodies develop under the soil surface associated with the roots of assorted Pinaceae species above 1,350 m in elevation, particularly Abies amabilis, A. procera, A. magnifica var. shastensis, Pinus monticola, or Tsuga mertensiana, August through September^, (Castellano), single to gregarious or cespitose, underground or emergent, Cascade Mountains at elevations 1200-1800m, (Cazares), emergent from heavy duff under conifers, especially hemlock, during late summer in the Cascade Mountains of Washington, (Smith), type emergent from heavy duff under hemlock, (Zeller), summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  not obtainable^ (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (8)9-15(20) x 4-6 microns, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, inamyloid, pale green to olive in KOH, walls up to 0.5 microns thick^; basidia 2-spored to 4-spored, 26-40 x 7-11 microns, cylindric to clavate, colorless, thin-walled; cystidia scattered, 25-50 x 4-14 microns, fusoid-ventricose, colorless to pale olive in KOH, thin-walled, lacking incrustations; peridial epicutis "initially differentiated as a palisade of cylindric to clavate dermatocystidia 15-30(70) x 3-10 microns", colorless to pale yellow in KOH, becoming a disrupted turf over the gleba but remaining a palisade over percurrent columellar apices; clamp connections absent, (Cazares), spores (10)11-14(15) x 4-5.5(6) microns, narrowly oval to nearly oblong, smooth, weakly pseudoamyloid, yellowish in KOH, wall thickened up to 0.5 microns; basidia 4-spored, 26-33 x 9-11 microns, clavate; cystidia scattered, 38-50 x 9-14 microns, fusoid-ventricose, tapered gradually to acute apex, colorless to yellow in KOH, a few with yellow inclusions in KOH, thin-walled, not incrusted; epicutis "a palisade of thin-walled dermatocystidia over the youngest peridia"; clamp connections absent, (Smith), spores 10-12.5 x 4.5-5.5 microns, oblong-elliptic, ochraceous brown, (Zeller)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'red'
SIMILAR  Gastroboletus turbinatus is similar but has a distinct cap and wider spores, (Bessette).
SOURCES  Cazares(4), Smith(29) (as Truncocolumella rubra), Castellano(1)*, Bessette(3)*, Arora(1) (as Truncocolumella rubra), Zeller(7) (as Truncocolumella rubra), Trappe(13)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gastroboletus subalpinus  Trappe and Thiers^  Brittonia 21: 245. 1969;  Boletus subalpinus  (Trappe & Thiers) M. Nuhn, Manfr. Binder, A.F.S. Taylor, Halling, Hibbett  Fungal Biology 117 (7-8): 479-511. 2013  (proposed current name)
ENGLISH NAMES  gastroid king-bolete
NOTES  Also listed in Truffles etc. category. Features include 1) a whitish to buff cap, 2) white flesh that does not turn blue, 3) tubes oriented at various angles and often covered by a membrane extending down from cap, 4) whitish pores that finally darken to olive brown, 5) a short stem colored as the cap, and 6) development under the ground under mountain conifers, often with partial exposure at maturity.^ It is common among false truffles in the Pacific Northwest (Trappe(13)). The species belongs in a bolete genus. Boletus subalpinus is a homotypic synonym in MycoBank, accessed February 18, 2018, and the currently accepted name in Species Fungorum, accessed the same day. RANGE Gastroboletus subalpinus is fairly common a higher elevations in CA, OR, and Washington, (Bessette), There is a collection from Washington at the University of Washington by P. Matheny in 1996, but Castellano(1)(1999) gives sites only in CA and OR and says "not known from Washington". The type is from Cloud Cap Oregon, not far from Washington.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  5-12cm, convex becoming flat to depressed or somewhat irregular; "whitish to buff or pale tan", sometimes darkening somewhat when old; "smooth to slightly velvety, often pitted and/or wrinkled, not viscid but often covered with dirt and debris"; margin extending down over tubes as thin white membrane that often disappears or breaks up when old^, (Arora), 6-10cm, convex becoming flat to deeply depressed to flat-convex when mature, bolete-like in general appearance, often highly pitted and wrinkled and irregular in outline; "warm buff" to "light buff" to "pale olive buff" to "pinkish cinnamon", unchanging when old or darkening to near "clay color" to "buckthorn brown" (colors from Ridgway); dry to moist, bald to velvety to subtomentose, typically covered with soil and debris; peridium on lateral and undersides of spore-producing area "white, thin, velvety, persistent except for scattered, circular ruptures" over mouths of occasional tubes when old, (Thiers)
FLESH  thick, soft; "white or with yellow to olive discolorations", not turning blue when bruised but sometimes staining pinkish^, (Arora), 0.3-1.5cm thick at disc, tapering to margin, soft; white or on some fruitbodies "with sordid yellowish or olivaceous zones", in some fruitbodies "slowly staining pinkish to grayish lavender when cut", in stem white, unchanging, (Thiers)
PORES  pallid or whitish becoming grayish yellow to buff and finally darkening to olive-brown, not turning blue when bruised; tubes occasionally vertical especially near stem but mostly at various angles, colored more or less like pores^, (Arora), less than 1mm across, colored like tubes, unchanging when bruised; tube layer 1-3cm thick, but markedly decreased in thickness in both directions from thickest area, depressed to seceded from stem, strongly projecting beyond upper cap surface but then covered by the thin, pallid, lateral outer covering; occasionally tubes vertical near stem but mostly curved and oriented 20 degrees from vertical, or angled upward near edges of fruitbody, "dark olive buff" to "pale olive buff", when old becoming "buffy citrine" to "citrine drab", (Ridgway colors), (Thiers), pores round to angular, (Bessette)
STEM  2-6cm x 2-5cm, narrowing downward or sometimes equal or even swollen in lower part, solid; white to buff, sometimes darkening to tan when old, base sometimes orange-yellow; dry, often dirty^, (Arora), 2-5cm x 2-4.5cm at top, base up to 3-4cm wide; subventricose [more or less wide in middle] or narrowing downward, occasionally "equal to bulbous at the base"; dirty white to "warm buff" to "light buff" in some, the base "apricot yellow" (Ridgway colors); dry, but typically covered with adhering debris, bald to velvety to subtomentose with the top reticulate when gleba has seceded, (Thiers), no annulus (Bessette)
ODOR  mild to farinaceous^ (Thiers)
TASTE  mild, pleasant^, (Thiers)
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single to gregarious "in soil or duff under mountain conifers in late spring and summer", developing underground but often partially exposed when mature^, (Arora), single to gregarious, underground or partly emerging, under Pinus albicaulis or Abies magnifica and possibly other conifers of montane and subalpine forests, "Commonly found late spring and summer", (Thiers), developing under soil surface associated with the roots of various Pinaceae above 1550m elevation, particularly Abies magnifica (Red Fir), Pinus albicaulis (Whitebark Pine), and Tsuga mertensiana (Mountain Hemlock), September and October, (Castellano), with Pinus albicaulis, Pinus contorta, Abies, Tsuga mertensiana, at relatively high elevations to the tree line; July to October, (Trappe, M.(3)), spring, summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  unobtainable^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-16(18) x 4.5-6(8) microns, elliptic to spindle-shaped, smooth, yellowish to brown^, (Arora), spores 10-16(18) x 4.5-6(8) microns, elliptic, oblong to subovate [somewhat oval], "occasionally irregularly constricted near middle", smooth, light yellow to ochraceous in Melzer's reagent and KOH, moderately thick-walled; basidia 4-spored, 40-48 x 8-13 microns, clavate, colorless and guttulate in KOH, thin-walled, brachybasidioles 25-40 x 7-12 microns, similar in shape and appearance to basidia; cystidia "often abundant when young, especially on the pores, apparently collapsing and appearing infrequently with age, single or sometimes clustered", 24-75 x 7-10 microns, subcylindric to clavate to tapered, colorless in KOH and Melzer's reagent, thin-walled; epicutis of peridium on top of fruitbody of densely interwoven, much branched hyphae 4-15 microns wide, with erect, tapered, blunt-tipped terminal cells 25-45 x 7-12 microns, sparsely scattered over surface; clamp connections absent, (Thiers)
NAME ORIGIN  means "subalpine, growing below the timber line" (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Boletus barrowsii and Boletus edulis are somewhat similar, but those species have vertical tubes.
SOURCES  Thiers(7), Arora(1), Bessette(3)*, Castellano(1)*, Trappe, M.(1)*, Trappe, M.(3)*, Both(1), Desjardin(6)*, Trappe(13)
30
FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gastroboletus turbinatus  (Snell) A.H. Sm. and Singer^  Brittonia 11: 208-210. 1959
ENGLISH NAMES  gastroid bolete, bogus bolete
NOTES  Also listed in Truffles etc. category. Features include 1) an irregular to top-shaped cap that is yellowish brown to reddish brown sometimes with yellowish or reddish tinges, 2) yellow flesh that turns blue when exposed, 3) yellowish tubes that are irregular and not vertically oriented, 4) angular pores that are yellow or tinged orange and turn blue when bruised, 5) a short stem that is yellow with reddish tinges and turns blue when bruised, and 6) growth under conifers, often partly buried.^ The taxon will likely be recombined as one or more species in a bolete genus. Var. flammeus, known from Idaho, grows on the ground under fir and has a pale golden cap with red blotches, bright red pores when young, and shorter spores 11-14 x 7-9 microns, (Bessette). Var. flammeus may occur elsewhere in the Pacific Northwest and other varieties are not well documented. Among false truffles in the Pacific Northwest, G. turbinatus is common, (Trappe(13)). RANGE Gastroboletus turbinatus is found in WA, OR, and ID, and is apparently widely distributed in the western US and also found in MO, (Smith). It is also found in CA (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  2-5(8)cm across, convex to slightly depressed to irregular; usually brown to reddish brown to bright yellow with red or brown spots and stains; "dry, minutely velvety, often pitted or wrinkled"^, (Arora), 2-5(8)cm across, convex becoming flat or slightly depressed; bister to mummy-brown, golden-yellow or bay-red, or with a reddish to orange-salmon tinge at least along marginal area; dry, velvety, when old subtomentose [somewhat tomentose], (Smith)
FLESH  soft; yellow, typically bruising blue quickly; in stem base often reddish^, (Arora), flesh "yellow in the peridium, often partly pinkish in the stipe-columella, tending to stain blue when injured and later becoming red to brownish in these areas, especially on drying", (Smith)
PORES  "pinkish to reddish, orange, or yellow", typically turning blue quickly when bruised; tubes irregular and sinuous or oriented at various angles other than vertical, greenish yellow to yellow^, (Arora), round at first and about 0.5mm across, when old with large angular depressions reaching 1 mm across; tube chambers reaching 1-3cm or more in length but diminishing in length in both directions from the place where they are longest, "strongly projecting beyond the peridium or remaining covered by its extended thin portion, either strongly depressed or nearly free from the apex of the columella, more rarely broadly attached to the columella"; interior of gleba yellow and quickly turning blue on exposure, pores in some specimens becoming reddish at mouths when mature or when old, (Smith), 1-2 per mm, angular, "covered by a thin membrane at first", surface irregular and uneven; yellow, sometimes tinted orange, quickly turning blue when bruised; "tubes 1-3cm long, irregular and not vertically arranged", depressed near stem, (Bessette)
STEM  1-4(7)cm x 1-2.5cm, equal or narrowing at base, often protruding only a short distance below tubes, solid; "yellow or with reddish streaks or granules or colored like the cap", sometimes reddish when old and often reddish at base; dry^, (Arora), 1-2(7)cm x 0.8-2cm, equal or narrowing downward, often not projecting much below gleba, solid; unpolished in upper part, "yellow or more orange-yellow to reddish toward the base, turning blue when bruised, sometimes finally becoming reddish"; frequently finely rivulose [with river-like lines] "because of the cracking of a brownish to reddish layer, the layer finally breaking up into minute points, at times brown-furfuraceous in a narrow zone", stem finally becoming bald; no veil, (Smith)
ODOR  slight and insignificant^ (Smith)
TASTE  slight and insignificant^ (Smith)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Arora)
HABITAT  single to scattered or gregarious "on ground in woods or at their edges, often partially buried"^, (Arora), on humus under conifers, often Abies spp. (fir), along mountain paths and roadsides, July through September, (Smith), summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  not obtainable^ (Arora)
MICROSCOPIC  spores (9.5)13.5-18 x 6.5-9.5 microns, elliptic to spindle-shaped, smooth, brown or golden^, (Arora), spores (9.5)13.5-18(20) x (5.5)6.5-9.5 microns, elliptic to oblong or slightly oval, honey-colored, often with a fulvous tinge, "walls simple at first, later thickening and becoming 3-layered", "apex obtuse to acute and the wall there either continuous or showing a slight pore"; basidia mostly 4-spored but sometimes with 1, 2 or 3 spores, 35-38 x 9-14 microns, "rather voluminous, vesiculose-pedicellate to clavate", colorless or yellow in KOH, basidioles scattered, variable in size or shape, about 33 x 9 microns, mostly somewhat clavate, colorless; "pseudoparaphyses similar in size and shape to the basidioles but without protoplasmic content and hence not likely to produce spores"; cystidia rare but present in interior as well as pores, 35-60 x 9.5-17 microns, ventricose-mucronate to more often ventricose in middle and ampullaceous-pedicellate, often with a long thin neck, colorless, later either colorless or golden yellow to brownish, apex usually obtuse; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means "shaped like a spinning top, obconical, turbinate", (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Gastroboletus ruber lacks a distinct cap and has narrower spores.
SOURCES  Smith(29), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Miller(14)*, Castellano(2)*, Trudell(4)*, Thiers(7), Both(1), Trappe(13)
