FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gastroboletus vividus  Trappe & Castellano^  Mycotaxon 75: 158. 2000
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Also listed in Truffles etc. category. Features include 1) a bright yellow and red cap, 2) pale yellow flesh that stains slowly red where exposed, 3) yellow pores that turn olive and then red-orange to red, 4) tubes that are olive and often contorted, 5) abruptly bulbous yellow stem that is red-furfuraceous at top, 6) growth underground or partially underground under conifers, and 7) microscopic characters including inamyloid spores on (2-)4-spored basidia.^ The species will likely be recombined into a bolete genus. The description derived from Trappe(2) except where specified. It is infrequent among false truffles in the Pacific Northwest (Trappe(13)). RANGE Gastroboletus vividus is found from southern OR Cascade Mountains to northern CA, (Trappe(13)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  Melzer's reagent nonreactive on fresh or dry flesh
CAP  3-5 x 3.5-6.5(8)cm, like a bolete but with cap margins strongly turned up when mature; cap surface "moderate yellow with red areas or red blushed overall"; dry, felty
FLESH  "pale yellow, very slowly staining pale red where exposed", with narrow, olive to red zones at tube attachment and immediately beneath cap surface; in stem red at top, pale yellow in lower part, unchanging when exposed
PORES  0.5-2.0mm, round to elliptic; brilliant yellow at first but soon becoming olive and by maturity red-orange to dark red, not blueing where bruised; tube layer 1-2cm thick, tubes "aligned from slightly below horizontal to nearly vertically upward, often contorted", adnate-seceding, olive
STEM  2-3cm x 1-3cm, abruptly bulbous below tube layer; brilliant yellow to sordid yellow; red-furfuraceous at top
ODOR  not distinctive
TASTE  not distinctive
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  growing underground or partially underground under mature conifers (Abies concolor, A. magnifica var. shastensis, Pinus contorta and Tsuga mertensiana), July to September, (Trappe(2)), above 1650m elevation (Castellano), summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores (11)13-18(22) x 6-7(8) microns, fusoid or occasionally oboval, smooth, inamyloid^ ("in Melzer's reagent pale yellow singly and brown-yellow in mass"), "in KOH golden yellow singly and bright brown-yellow in mass"; basidia (2-)4-spored, 30-45 x 9-11 microns, clavate; cystidia absent; cap cuticle a loose tangle of hyphae, thin-walled hyphae 4-8 microns wide at septa, the cells mostly slightly inflated; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means "brightly colored" (Trappe(2))
SIMILAR  Gastroboletus amyloideus Thiers of California is somewhat less brightly colored and has amyloid spores, (Trappe(2)).
SOURCES  Trappe(2), Castellano(1)*, Trappe(13)

FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gastrosuillus imbellus  (Trappe) Thiers^  Mem. N.Y. bot. Gdn 49: 358. 1989;  Gastroboletus imbellus  Trappe  Brittonia 21: 249-251. 1969
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Also listed in Truffles etc. category. There is doubt about whether this is a distinct species - it is thought to be a distorted Suillus punctatipes. Gastrosuillus imbellus is characterized by 1) drab color, 2) persistent hanging veil remnants on cap margin, 3) short tubes oriented at an angle to vertical, 4) pores mostly blocked by folds and outgrowths of wall tissue, 5) short laterally attached stem-columella, 6) pungent-farinaceous odor, 7) slightly bitter taste, and 8) formation under the soil surface.^ The description is derived from Thiers(7) unless otherwise specified. RANGE Gastrosuillus imbellus is endemic to OR and known from a single site there, (Castellano).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^with 5% KOH, tube mouths turn dark brown, bases deep lilac, flesh near cap surface deep lilac
CAP  5cm across, convex, expanded, bolete-like in appearance except for lateral attachment of cap to stem; grayish yellow ground color, overlain by dark olivaceous fibrils to produce overall sordid gray appearance; moist but not viscid, cap surface with a few broad cracks that extend deeply into flesh, which is blushed rose where exposed in this way; margin with hanging ragged membranous veil remnants from a 0.2-0.3cm wide extension of the cap surface
FLESH  2cm thick at attachment of stem, soft; "white with scattered pale yellow stained areas", a rosy zone above the tubes and a 0.2cm thick olive-hygrophanous zone under the cap surface, "slowly and erratically staining light sordid brownish where cut"; in stem pallid with vinaceous blush near base and turning slowly sordid when cut
PORES  gleba (spore producing tissue) exposed and tubulose; pores 1-4 per mm, round, mostly blocked by folds and outgrowths of wall tissue; tubes oriented at about 40 degrees from vertical, decurrent, short (not over 0.2cm long), pale grayish olive
STEM  stem-columella 3cm long, 1.5cm at apex, equal except narrowing slightly at base, laterally attached to cap; dull yellowish at top, grading to sordid cream in middle part, to pale salmonaceous with copious dark brown stains at base; dry at top, "upper half with dark brown to blackish glandular dots which are minute singly but often coalesced into patches up to one-half mm broad"
ODOR  pungent-farinaceous
TASTE  slightly bitter
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single under Tsuga mertensiana and Abies amabilis, midsummer, (Thiers), "sporocarps beneath the soil surface associated with the roots of Abies grandis, A. lasiocarpa, Tsuga mertensiana, with an understory of Antennaria lanata, Fragaria sp., Pachistima myrsinites, Sorbus sitchensis, and Vaccinium sp. at 1,650m elevation", (Castellano), summer
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 7-10 x 2.5 microns, narrowly to broadly elliptic to obovate, smooth, pale yellow in Melzer's reagent, colorless in KOH, most with 1 or 2 large droplets and often with several smaller ones^; basidia 20-30 x 3-5(7) microns, colorless in KOH, brachybasidioles similar to basidia but shorter; cystidia 25-60 x 4-6 microns, fascicled, colorless to vinaceous to dark brown in KOH "with much brown, amorphous material deposited at the base of the clusters, cylindric to fusoid-ventricose or irregularly constricted"; epicutis of peridium in KOH of granulated pale brownish, thin-walled hyphae 2-6 microns wide, subcutis similar except hyphae 5-12 microns wide and much yellowish to pale vinaceous debris present, in Melzer's reagent epicuticular hyphae are yellowish and subcuticular hyphae more or less vinaceous stained; orange-brown pigment balls scattered throughout; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means "not beautiful" (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Suillus pungens of California is also drab in color and unpleasant in odor and taste, but has normal tubes and pores, (Thiers).
SOURCES  Thiers(7) (as Gastroboletus), Castellano(1) (as Gastroboletus), Both(1) (as Gastroboletus)

FAMILY  Suillaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gastrosuillus suilloides  (Thiers) Thiers^  Mem. N. Y. bot. Gdn 49: 358. 1989;  Gastroboletus suilloides  Thiers  Brittonia 21: 248. 1969;  Suillus suilloides (Thiers) Kretzer & T.D. Bruns  Mycologia 89(4): 589. 1997  (proposed new name)
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Also listed in the Boletes category. Features of Gastrosuillus suilloides include 1) a hypogeous to partly emergent fruitbody that is convex to nearly round, the surface yellowish brown to cinnamon brown or pinkish brown, dry, and bald becoming fibrillose-scaly or tomentose, 2) unchanging buff to cream context, 3) a spore mass with distorted greenish-yellow tubes, and small irregular pores of the same color, 4) an poorly developed stem-columella colored like the cap, 5) mild odor and taste, 6) growth under conifers at higher elevations, and 7) microscopic characters including fascicled darkly staining cystidia and spore shape that both suggest Suillus.^ The description is derived from Thiers(7). RANGE Gastrosuillus suilloides was described from CA but according to Noah Siegel (pers. comm.) has been recorded also from OR.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  2.5-5cm across when mature, "convex to subglobose to plano-convex during all stages of development"; peridium ' "ochraceous buff" to "cinnamon brown" to "buckthorn brown" to occasionally as dark as "mikado brown" to "sayal brown" ' when older, "unchanging when bruised"; dry, bald when young, "often becoming fibrillose to tomentose to appressed fibrillose scaly with age, sometimes becoming areolate with age"
FLESH  0.3-0.7cm thick, "ochraceous buff" to pale "cream color", "unchanging when exposed"
PORES  pores "small, irregular", greenish yellow to olive-yellow; gleba distinctly "tubulose", tubes typically exposed at maturity (but "arranged so as to make a spore deposit impossible"); colored like the pore surface, "unchanging when bruised or exposed"
STEM  stem-columella typically poorly developed, 0.5-1.0cm x 0.3-0.5cm, more or less colored as the cap during all stages of development; dry, tomentose to appressed-fibrillose
ODOR  not distinctive
TASTE  not distinctive
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  single to gregarious, "hypogeous to partially emergent", under conifers including Abies magnifica (California Red Fir) and Pinus contorta ssp. murrayana (Sierra Lodgepole Pine) in higher elevations of the Sierra Nevada of California, late summer
SPORE DEPOSIT  none
MICROSCOPIC  spores 6.4-12 x 3.5-4.5 microns, elliptic to inequilateral, some appearing oval or "occasionally more or less constricted at the midportion", "smooth, walls slightly thickened, sterigmal appendages oblique"; basidia 4-spored, 20-37 x 6-8 microns, "narrowly clavate to subcylindric, clavate to depressed at the center", colorless in KOH; brachybasidioles 15-23 x 4-8 microns, clavate; cystidia "abundant to numerous", 35-55 x 5-11 microns, "clavate to cylindric to subcylindric", "typically clustered and staining dark brown in KOH and Melzer's" as in Suillus; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means 'like Suillus'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Thiers(7) (colors in double quotation marks from Ridgway(1))

FAMILY  Gyroporaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Gyroporus castaneus  (Fr.) Quel.^  Enchir. Fung. p.161. 1886;  Boletus castaneus  Fr.
ENGLISH NAMES  chestnut bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a red-brown to yellow brown cap that is dry and velvety to smooth, 2) white unchanging flesh that is firm and brittle, 3) white to pale yellow pores and tubes neither of which turn blue, 4) a hollow stem colored like cap or slightly paler, 5) growth under hardwoods, 6) yellow spore deposit, and 7) microscopic characters including clamp connections.^ RANGE Gyroporus castaneus is found in CA, WA, and OR, (Thiers), from eastern Canada south to FL, west to CA, and Mexico, (Bessette), NS (Grund), and Europe (Arora). Collections from BC are deposited at the Pacific Forestry Centre.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^cap cuticle stains yellow then bleaches to white with application of KOH and is negative with FeSO4, flesh stains very pale brownish or is negative with application of KOH or FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  2.5-7(10)cm, convex to flat or shallowly depressed; "chestnut-brown to brown, cinnamon-brown, orange-brown, or rusty-tawny"; "dry, minutely hairy to smooth, sometimes with a delicate whitish bloom when young"^, (Arora), 3-10cm, rounded to broadly convex becoming nearly flat, sometimes slightly depressed, margin often split and flaring when old; chestnut to yellow-brown or orange-brown; dry, velvety-tomentose to nearly bald, (Bessette), 3-7cm, obtuse to convex, becoming broadly convex to flat-convex, flat, or depressed; brown to tan to pinkish brown; dry, bald to finely tomentose to pulverulent [powdery], often cracked or areolate-cracked [cracked like dried mud] when old; margin becoming cracked or split, (Thiers), sometimes appearing granulose-reticulate when old, (Smith)
FLESH  thick, firm; white, not turning blue when bruised^, (Arora), brittle; white, not staining blue, (Bessette), thick; white, unchanging when exposed, (Thiers)
PORES  white, becoming pale yellow when old, not turning blue; tubes the same color^, (Arora), 1-3 per mm, round; whitish to buff or yellowish, never pinkish or colored like the flesh; tube layer 0.5-0.8cm thick, (Bessette), 1-2 per mm, angular, radially elongate near top of stem, white becoming yellow at maturity, unchanging or darkening slightly when bruised; tube layer 0.3-0.5cm thick, shallow to deeply and broadly depressed around stem, colored as pores, (Thiers), 1-2 per mm, round to angular; tubes free and deeply sunken around stem, (Phillips), tubes free and always shorter towards stem (Lincoff(1)), tubes free or deeply depressed around stem, rarely adnate or subdecurrent, whitish becoming yellowish, unchanging when bruised or merely changing to brownish, pores not staining when bruised, (Smith)
STEM  3-9cm x 0.5-1(3)cm, more or less equal, sometimes wider in lower part, hollow or partially hollow at least when mature; "brown to tawny (colored more or less like cap or slightly paler)"; dry, uneven^, (Arora), 3-9cm x 0.6-1.6cm, equal or often swollen in middle or lower part, often narrowed at top and base, "brittle, stuffed with a soft pith, developing several cavities" or becoming hollow when old; colored like cap or slightly paler toward top; surface uneven, not reticulate; partial veil and annulus absent, (Bessette), 2-4 x 0.5-1.5cm, equal or widening downward from top, hollow in lower part; tan to brown to pinkish brown, similar to cap; dry, unpolished, bald to tomentose to velvety, (Thiers)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive (Bessette), hazelnut (Lincoff(1)), mild, slightly acidic (Miller)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single or in groups under hardwoods^, (Arora), single, scattered, or in groups under mixed conifers and hardwoods, (Bessette), summer and fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  pale yellow to yellow^, (Arora), pale yellow to buff (Bessette)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 8-12 x 4.5-6 microns, elliptic-oblong, smooth^, (Arora), spores 8-13 x 5-6 microns, elliptic to oval, smooth, colorless, (Bessette), spores 10-12.5(14) x 5-6 microns, elliptic, smooth, ochraceous in Melzer's reagent, colorless to pale yellow in KOH, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 30-35 x 8-14 microns, clavate, colorless to pale yellow in KOH; hymenial cystidia abundant, 30-45 x 7-11 microns, fusoid to obscurely fusoid-ventricose, colorless, thin-walled; cap cuticle a layer of cystidioid cells forming a tangled trichodermium; stem cuticle similar to cap cuticle; clamp connections present, (Thiers), spores lacking germ pore; clamp connections present, but many pseudoclamps also present, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  from Byzantine Greek "chestnut" (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Arora(1)*, Smith(35), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Breitenbach(3)*, Grund(11), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Hemileccinum subglabripes  (Peck) Halling^  in Halling, Fechner, Nuhn, Osmundson, Soytong, Arora, Binder & Hibbett, Aust. Syst. Bot. 28: 19 (2015);  Boletus subglabripes  Peck  Bull. N.Y. State Museum 2(8): 112. 1889;  Leccinum subglabripes  (Peck) Singer
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry smooth cap that is ochraceous to cinnamon to reddish brown, 2) whitish to pale yellow flesh that only rarely turns slightly bluish, 3) small round yellow pores that do not change when injured, 4) a yellowish stem that has tiny yellow scales but no reticulum and may have reddish or reddish brown tinges at the base, 5) growth under birch or other hardwoods, and 6) microscopic characters.^ Molecular data support segregation from Boletus as Hemileccinum subglabripes Halling. (Halling(6)). RANGE Bessette et al. give the distribution as eastern Canada to FL, west to MN and TX, but it has also been reported from WA, ID, (H. Thiers 1986, pers. comm. to L. Norvell), and Smith(4) cite it as very rare in the Pacific Northwest. The eastern distribution specifically includes NS (Grund).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains grayish olive-green with application of FeSO4 (Bessette)
CAP  4.5-10cm, convex becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old, sometimes broadly umbonate; "chestnut, ocher, cinnamon, or reddish brown"; "smooth to slightly wrinkled"^, (Bessette), 4.5-10cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex or broadly umbonate; yellow to ochraceous to clay color or more rarely dull cinnamon; dry to moist, bald, dull, often pitted or somewhat rugose [wrinkled], (Smith(35)), "light brown to rich cinnamon, yellow-brown, or reddish brown"; "dry, glabrous to slightly viscid when wet", (Phillips)
FLESH  pale yellow to whitish, rarely turning blue slightly when cut or bruised^, (Bessette), thick, up to 2cm, firm; whitish to pale yellow, occasionally irregular areas changing to olive-buff or greenish yellow when old where damaged, typically not changing when injured but rarely slightly bluish; in stem pale bright yellow except whitish in base, cortex sometimes staining reddish when old, (Smith(35))
PORES  2 per mm, round, yellow when fresh, duller or slightly greenish yellow when old; tube layer 0.5-1.6cm thick^, (Bessette), about 2 per mm, round, not stuffed when young; yellow, unchanging when injured; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, deeply and broadly depressed around stem, yellow, (Smith(35)) 
STEM  5-10cm x 1-2cm, nearly equal, solid; yellowish with occasional reddish or reddish brown tinges on lower part; "minutely scurfy with a thin coating of tiny yellow scales, dots, or points, not reticulate; partial veil and annulus absent"^, (Bessette), 5-10cm x 1-2cm, equal to ventricose, narrowing slightly toward top or base, solid; pale to bright yellow, occasionally with reddish stains at base; dry, furfuraceous to scabrous or fibrillose, never reticulate, (Smith(35))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  mild to slightly acidic^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  scattered on ground under hardwoods, especially birch, occasionally under conifers^, (Bessette)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette), olive to olive-ochraceous brown (Smith(35))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-17 x 3-5 microns, narrowly fusoid [spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^, (Bessette), spores 11-14 x 3-5 microns, narrowly fusoid, smooth, pale greenish yellow revived in KOH, yellowish in Melzer's, wall less than 0.2 microns thick with no apical pore seen; basidia 4-spored, 18-26 x 8-10 microns, clavate, colorless to yellowish in KOH; pleurocystidia rare to scattered, 32-54 x 8-15 microns, fusoid-ventricose, smooth, thin-walled, apex acute, contents colorless to yellow revived in KOH, cheilocystidia numerous, 20-32 x 8-12 microns, fusoid to fusoid-ventricose, colorless to yellow in KOH; cap cuticle "a compact trichodermium the elements of which have the distal 2-5 cells inflated to sphaerocyst proportions and some so closely packed together as to produce a cellular layer, the cells (6)10-24 microns in diameter, more or less isodiametric and with yellow walls as revived in KOH or in Melzer's"; stem cuticle has hymenium present in patches, caulocystidia 35-60 x 10-30 microns, soon colorless in KOH mounts, smooth, thin-walled, (Smith(35)), spores (11)12-14(17) x 3-3.5(5) microns, (Phillips)
NAME ORIGIN  means "with nearly smooth stem"^ (Both)
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus subglabripes), Smith(35) (as Boletus subglabripes), Smith(4) (as Boletus subglabripes), Both(1) (as Boletus subglabripes), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus subglabripes), Grund(11) (as Boletus subglabripes), Halling(6)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Hortiboletus rubellus  (Krombh.) Simonini, Vizzini & Gelardi^  in Vizzini, Index Fungorum 244: 1 2015;  Boletus rubellus  Krombh.  Naturgetr. Abbild. Beschr. Schwamme (Prague) 5: 4. 1836;  Xerocomus rubellus  (Krombh.) Quel.;  Xerocomellus rubellus  (Krombh.) Sutara
ENGLISH NAMES  ruby bolete
NOTES  Features include a red, dry, velvety cap that often becomes finely cracked, yellow flesh that turns slowly and slightly blue when cut, small, yellow pores then turn somewhat blue when bruised, more or less cylindric stem that is yellow at the top and reddish in the lower part, and growth near hardwoods.^ Hortiboletus rubellus is found in North America, Europe, North Africa, and Asia, (Breitenbach), and the United Kingdom (Bon). The range in North America is given by Bessette(3) as "eastern North America west to Minnesota", but Trudell(4) say that several Pacific Northwest collections fit H. rubellus well. Trudell(4) say, "There is a good chance that boletes in this group have been introduced into our area. They often occur during summer in grassy areas, mossy lawns, or along the edges of trails, always near trees such as oaks, cottonwood, willow, and basswood or linden." RANGE Hortiboletus rubellus [or something close] has been collected from Seattle, WA (S. Trudell, pers. comm.). 
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  flesh stains dull orange with KOH, and olive-green with FeSO4, (Bessette)
CAP  2-8cm across, convex, becoming nearly flat when old, margin even; dark red when young, fading to brick-red when old; dry, velvety-subtomentose, often becoming finely rimose [cracked]^ when mature, (Bessette(3)), 3-5cm across, hemispheric - bell-shaped when young, later convex to flat, somewhat undulating when old; "strikingly blood-red when young, later fading and brown-red"; dry, finely tomentose, (Breitenbach), 3-8cm across, broadly convex; brick-red; dry, velvety, finally areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Smith), scarlet red when young, becoming dull olivaceous red when old, margin often yellowish; dry and velvety, finally bald, and often areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Phillips), 5-7cm across, scarlet to vinaceous red; smooth, (Bon)
FLESH  "bright yellow, slowly staining bluish green on exposure"; in stem "yellow, slowly staining bluish green, reddish orange in the base"^, (Bessette(3)), thick, soft; yellowish, blueing slightly when cut, (Breitenbach), pale, only slowly tinged blue (Bon)
PORES  1 to 2 per mm, angular, thick-walled, "yellow, staining bluish green when bruised"; tube layer 0.6-1cm deep, typically depressed when mature^, (Bessette(3)), lemon yellow to gold-yellow when young, "later with an olive tint, bluing somewhat when touched"; tubes 0.5-1cm deep, greenish yellow, the layer "somewhat notched and sometimes decurrent as thin ridges", (Breitenbach), small; pale yellow, (Bon)
STEM  3-8cm x 0.5-1.3cm, narrowing downward or nearly equal, often with a narrowed base; yellow at top, "punctate with dull reddish orange dots and points" from the base to the top, "darkening to brown where handled"^, (Bessette(3)), 4-10cm x 0.7-1.5(2)cm, cylindric to fusiform-clavate; light yellow at top, increasingly red to brown-red downward, base and mycelium chrome yellow, blueing slightly when bruised; punctate at top, increasingly longitudinally fibrillose downward, (Breitenbach), equal; bright yellow at top, shading to bright rose red or scarlet in lower part, with yellow basal mycelium, (Phillips), 6cm x 1cm, yellow, spotted red in lower part; smooth, (Bon)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3), Breitenbach), pleasant (Phillips)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3)), mild, sourish, (Breitenbach), slightly soapy (Phillips)
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette(3))
HABITAT  single, scattered, or in groups on the ground in hardwood forests and mixed woods, especially under oak and beech; July to October^, (Bessette(3)), single to gregarious, in mixed hardwood forests, parks, gardens, and floodplain forests, primarily on calcareous soils; summer to fall, (Breitenbach)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown (Bessette, Phillips), yellow-brown, (Breitenbach)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 10-13 x 4-5 microns, "elliptic, smooth, pale brown"^; cap cuticle a trichodermium of long, tubular, tapered, colorless hyphae, 5-12 microns wide and not conspicuously inflated, with dingy ochraceous content in Melzer's reagent, (Bessette(3)), spores 9.5-12.5 x 4.2-6 microns, elliptic, smooth, thick-walled, yellowish; basidia 4-spored, 35-45 x 9-13 microns, clavate, without basal clamp connection; pleurocystidia and cheilocystidia 35-62 x 8.5-11 microns, cylindric-lageniform, some with faintly yellow contents; cap cuticle a trichoderm of mostly erect hyphae 6-12 microns wide, "some with reddish incrustation", septa without clamp connections, (Breitenbach), cap cuticle a trichodermium with hyphae 5-11 microns wide, walls smooth to minutely roughened, end cells cylindric to subcapitate, 8-25 microns broad where enlarged, (Smith), spores up to 12-13 microns long, thick-walled; "cap cuticle composed of erect septate hyphae with tapered ends and some encrusted walls", (Trudell)
NAME ORIGIN  'rubellus' is diminutive of 'ruber', Latin for 'red'
SIMILAR  Xerocomellus 'diffractus' and Boletus truncatus are less brightly colored, (Trudell(4)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)* (as Boletus rubellus), Breitenbach(3)* (as Xerocomus rubellus), Trudell(4)* (as Boletus rubellus), Bon(2) (as Xerocomus), Phillips(1)* (as Boletus rubellus), Smith(4) (as Boletus rubellus), Courtecuisse(1)* (as Boletus rubellus), Bessette(2)* (as Boletus rubellus), Bacon(1)* (as Boletus rubellus), Buczacki(1)* (as Xerocomus)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum atrostipitatum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 155-157. 1966
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dull orange to pinkish tan or brownish cap (pale and rather dull colored) that is often patchy when old, 2) flesh that stains pink then purple-gray to blackish, and with FeSO4 turns bluish gray, 3) pores that are pale when young when young, becoming dingier when old, unchanging or staining olive to brownish when bruised, 4) a stem with dense black scabers when young and when mature, and 5) growth with birch.^ The online Species Fungorum, accessed February 12, 2018, considered this taxon a synonym of Leccinum versipelle (Fr. & Hok) Snell, but MycoBank, accessed the same day, maintained them separately. RANGE The distribution is eastern Canada to MN, CO to WA, southern range unknown, (Bessette), AK (Dick(1)), and NS (Grund(11)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh bluish gray with FeSO4; flesh bluish with NH4OH, (Smith)
CAP  6-16cm, convex, margin at first with thin flaps of tissue, at least when young; "dull orange to tan or brownish"; dry to slightly viscid, "fibrillose, breaking up to form downy patches or small scales" when old^, (Bessette), 6-13(18)cm, hemispheric to broadly convex; pale dull orange-buff, "slowly becoming pale orange brownish and finally a dingy pinkish tan", dry, appressed-fibrillose, "becoming obscurely to distinctly appressed-squamulose by maturity, the outer fibrils often grayish", the squamules finally merely brownish; margin exceeding tubes by 0.5cm and this band soon breaking up into segments, (Smith)
FLESH  "white, staining pinkish, then purple-gray to blackish when exposed", especially where cap joins stem^, (Bessette), "white, when cut soon vinaceous-buff and this progressing to violaceous-fuscous", in stem white, color change the same but sometimes staining blue-green in addition toward the base, (Smith)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round, "whitish to buff or pale gray when young, becoming dingier in age, unchanging or staining olive to brownish when bruised"; tube layer 1-1.8cm thick^, (Bessette), minute, adnate-seceding; "staining olive to olive-brown and if severely bruised avellaneous or darker"; tube layer 1-1.5cm thick, "olivaceous-pallid when young, then dingy honey-brown and finally wood-brown", (Smith)
STEM  8-15cm x 1-2.5cm, nearly equal or widening downward, solid; dense layer of scabers that are black when fruitbody young and when mature, on whitish to dingy tan ground color, blue or blue green stains occasionally on lower part; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette), 8-15cm x 1-2.5cm at top, up to 4cm wide at base, clavate [club-shaped] becoming equal when old, solid, hard, fibrous; "coarse black ornamentation in the form of points and squamules from button stages to maturity and from base to apex, with a cottony-fibrillose layer beneath this", base may stain bluish green when injured, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  single to scattered under hardwoods, usually birch, July to September^, (Bessette), summer, fall
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette), dull yellow-brown (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-17 x 4-5 microns, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^; end cells on cap cuticle tubular, yellowish, lacking orange brown granules in Melzer's; caulocystidia clavate to fusoid-ventricose (Bessette), spores 13-17 x 4-5 microns, smooth, subfusiform, pale yellow brown in KOH and not changing much in Melzer's reagent, wall slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 8-12 microns wide, colorless in KOH; pleurocystidia "scattered, 30-40 X 8-12 microns, fusoid-ventricose, with dull yellow-brown content when revived in KOH or in Melzer's, smooth, thin-walled", cheilocystidia "26-35 X 6-10 microns, fusoid-ventricose to clavate, ochraceous in KOH"; cap cuticle of tangled filaments, in KOH the hyphae with yellow to colorless content, when revived in Melzer's yellowish without pigment globules but content granular to homogeneous, hyphae "with cells tubular to somewhat inflated, mostly 6-11 microns wide, some rather short, finally disarticulating, the end cells tubular to either narrowly clavate or tapered to apex, walls smooth to minutely asperulate"; caulocystidia "40-120 X 10-25 microns, clavate to fusoid-ventricose, content bister in KOH"; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  means 'with black stem'
SIMILAR  Leccinum versipelle has a brighter orange cap that is less scaly when old, a darker pore surface when young, flesh that stains bright green with FeSO4, and different caulocystidia, (Bessette). L. versipelle is less associated with birch, has a less patchy cap at maturity, and has different caulocystidia, (Smith).
SOURCES  Smith(35), Bessette(3)*, Dick(1), Grund(11)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum aurantiacum  (Bull.) Gray^  Nat. Arr. Brit. Pl. I: 646. 1821
ENGLISH NAMES  red-capped scaber-stalk, aspen scaberstalk, orange-capped bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a brick-red cap, 2) flesh that turns reddish before becoming bluish black, and 3) the development of pigment globules in cells of the cap cuticle hyphae in Melzer's reagent. This name was formerly applied to several similar species, and its presence in North America is disputed.^ See Kuo(5) (accessed July 10, 2013) under "Leccinum: Uncertain Taxa" for a more detailed discussion. Trudell(4) comment in relation to the Pacific Northwest (Latin names italicized) "A large, often abundant species that has been called Leccinum aurantiacum occurs in our conifer forests. That name, however, is incorrect since L. aurantiacum has been shown to be associated with hardwoods. Our species may be closer to L. vulpinum described from Europe.". RANGE Leccinum aurantiacum is said to be "widely distributed across the northern United States and Canada, southern limits unknown", (Bessette). Various observers give a range that includes CA (rare), (Thiers(1)), AK (Dick(1)), CO (Lincoff(2)), WA (reported by numerous observers), OR (R. Bishop, pers. comm.), ID (E. Butler, pers. comm.), MT (L. Evans, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh stains very pale blue with application of FeSO4 (Bessette)
CAP  5-20.5cm, convex to broadly convex, to nearly flat when old; "orange to brick-red, rusty red, or reddish brown"; dry, appressed-fibrillose when young, often bald when old; "margin with thin flaps of sterile tissue when young"^, (Bessette), 8-15cm, convex to broadly convex or flat-convex; bright rust brown to brick red; "dry, dull, uneven, or roughened, finely to matted-fibrillose when young", unchanging or appearing bald when older, "sometimes finely and obscurely areolate"; margin with conspicuous sterile band, (Thiers(1)), bright to dull ferruginous-red but often with a pallid area where covered by debris; dry and uneven, roughened or appressed-fibrillose, when old more or less bald and subviscid, (Smith(35)), red to reddish orange-brown or dark reddish brown; fibrillose, (Trudell)
FLESH  "white, slowly staining pinkish to wine-red then gradually darkening to purple-gray or blackish when exposed", especially at junction between cap and stem^, (Bessette), 2-3cm thick, white when first exposed, changing to vinaceous to near flesh color, then gray to bluish gray or black, (Thiers(1)), white, when cut slowly staining vinaceous then grayish and then slowly fuscous, in the stem when cut "pallid at first but staining to fuscous in the upper part, sometimes staining caerulean blue in the base and in places strongly reddish to red-brown", (Smith(35)), whitish, "changes slightly or more distinctly to grayish or blackish, sometimes after going through a vinaceous gray phase", (Trudell)
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "whitish when young, becoming dingier and grayish to olive-buff to pale olive-brown in age, unchanging or staining olive, brownish or burgundy-brown when cut or bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick^, (Bessette), 1-2 per mm, angular; "staining olive to olive brown to vinaceous brown when bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, adnate to depressed when young, becoming deeply and narrowly depressed when old, when young pale olive, darkening to olive drab or becoming brown at maturity, (Thiers(1)), "when lightly bruised pores staining olive to olive-brown but where severely bruised stained avellaneous or darker", (Smith(35))
STEM  10-16cm x 2-3cm, equal or widening downward, solid; "whitish, with whitish to buff scabers that darken to orange-brown to reddish brown and finally blackish brown, at least over the lower portion, occasionally with blue-green or yellowish stains on the lower portion"; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 10-17cm x 2-3cm at top, clavate [club-shaped] to ventricose [wider in middle], becoming equal, often narrowed at base and top, solid; whitish to white, squamules pallid at first, black or dark brown when old, occasionally staining blue in base; dry, (Thiers(1)), with black scabers, base blueing, (Trudell)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette)
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  scattered or in groups under aspen or pine, July to September^, (Bessette), summer and fall (Bacon)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette, Thiers), dark yellow-brown to dull cinnamon (Smith(35))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-18 x 3.5-5 microns, subfusiform [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^; end cells of cap cuticle "narrowly clavate to tubular or slightly tapered, content orange-brown and forming globules of various sizes in Melzer's solution"; caulocystidia clavate to cylindric, (Bessette), spores 12-13.5 x 3.5-4.5 microns, (up to 28 microns long from 1-spored basidia), fusoid to subelliptic, smooth, pale ochraceous in KOH, yellow brown in Melzer's reagent, thin-walled; basidia 1-spored to 4-spored, 30-55 x 9-11 microns, clavate, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered, deeply embedded, 30-40 x 9-11 microns, clavate to fusoid, colorless in KOH, thin-walled; cap cuticle "a tangled trichoderm, pale ochraceous in KOH, hyphae 7-11 microns wide, considerable disarticulation of cells noted, walls often asperulate, pigment globules formed when mounted in Melzer's reagent, terminal cells not strongly differentiated, no short intermediate cells present"; caulocystidia "38-48 X 8-13 microns, dark brown in KOH, clavate, with large fusoid-ventricose cells interspersed"; clamp connections absent, (Thiers), spores 13-16(18) x 3.5-4.5(5) microns, subfusiform, smooth; basidia 4-spored, 8-9 microns broad, clavate; pleurocystidia rare to scattered, 35-60 x 8-12 microns, apices obtuse, cheilocystidia 18-26 x 5-8(10) microns, clavate to subfusoid; cap cuticle "of appressed interwoven hyphae 5-12(15) microns wide, cells elongate (mostly more than 5 times as long as broad), walls thin, smooth, some minutely roughened, the cells disarticulating to some extent; the end-cells narrowly clavate to tubular or near apex slightly tapered", in Melzer's the content orange-brown and forming globules often filling the cells; caulocystidia mostly clavate, 9-30 microns wide, a few cylindric, smooth, (Smith(35))
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin, "golden orange"^, (Lincoff(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum discolor has duller cap colors (dull orange brown to dull cinnamon), and lacks orange-brown pigment globules in the cap cuticle hyphae when revived in Melzer's reagent, (Bessette). Leccinum insigne has flesh that stains bluish gray to blackish without an intervening reddish or pinkish phase, and grows under birch as well as aspen, (Bessette). L. insigne is ornamented by maturity with black scabers, but Leccinum aurantiacum may not reach this state till old age; hyphae of the cap cuticle of L. aurantiacum are relatively smooth in contrast to those of L. insigne, (Smith(35)). Leccinum idahoense has a dark liver-brown granulose-areolate cap, is very slow change to gray when the flesh is cut, and has a relatively pale cinnamon spore deposit, (Smith(37)).
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Thiers(13), Bessette(3)*, Trudell(4), Smith(35), Phillips(1)*, Lincoff(2)*, Lincoff(1)*, Miller(14)*, Courtecuisse(1)*, McKnight(1)*, Dick(1), Smith(37), Bacon(1)*, Buczacki(1)*, Kuo(5)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum brunneum  Thiers^  Mycologia 63: 269. 1971
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dull dark brown cap with marginal flaps of tissue, 2) flesh that turns fuscous with no intermediate reddish phase, and sometimes becomes blue in base, 3) white pores that become pale olive and bruise brown, 4) stem with scabers that are white to darker, and 5) growth under aspen.^ Note that this is not the same as Leccinum insigne var. brunneum Thiers, which was described from California in the same paper. The description derived from Thiers(1) except where specified. RANGE L. brunneum was described from CA but was also reported from ID by Dr. H. Thiers who described it (pers. comm. to L. Norvell, 1986).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  no distinctive reactions observed^ (Thiers(13))
CAP  6-10cm, convex becoming broadly convex to cushion-shaped to irregular in outline; dark brown ("bister" to "warm sepia" to "Prout's brown", colors from Ridgway), sometimes somewhat paler brown when old ("cinnamon-brown" to "Verona brown" to "snuff brown"), drying dark dull brown; dry, but may be viscid or subviscid in wet weather, dull, bald to finely subtomentose [somewhat tomentose] or obscurely appressed-fibrillose, occasionally becoming somewhat fibrillose when old; margin with conspicuous sterile flaps of tissue
FLESH  1-2.5cm thick, "white when first exposed, changing to fuscous with no reddish intermediate phase, fuscous color sometimes fading after prolonged exposure"; in stem, white when first exposed, becoming fuscous directly as in cap, sometimes becoming blue in base
PORES  up to 1mm, angular, colored as tubes; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed, white when young becoming pale olive when mature, staining brown when bruised, color fading in some after bruising
STEM  8-15cm x 1.5-3(4)cm at top, club-shaped, solid; dry, white ground color, covered with small squamules that are white at first becoming darker with maturity
ODOR  mild
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  unknown (Thiers)
HABITAT  gregarious to scattered on ground under aspen
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown
MICROSCOPIC  spores (14)15.5-19.5 x 4-6 microns, fusoid to subcylindric [nearly cylindric] to subelliptic [nearly elliptic], smooth, some dextrinoid, in KOH ochraceous, walls moderately thick^; basidia 4-spored, 30-35 x 7-11 microns, clavate to pear-shaped, colorless in KOH; hymenial cystidia scattered to abundant, often inconspicuous, 35-50 x 8-12 microns, fusoid to fusoid-ventricose to clavate, some staining pale brown in KOH and dark reddish brown in Melzer's reagent; cap cuticle a trichodermium of tangled hyphal tips, pale ochraceous in KOH, tawny to russet in Melzer's reagent, hyphae 11-15 microns wide, walls sometimes asperulate, pigment globules not forming when mounted in Melzer's reagent, "terminal cells relatively long and narrow, tapered somewhat at the tip"; caulocystidia "24-37 x 7-12 microns, clavate to fusoid to clavate with elongated, tapering apices", typically dark brown, but sometimes colorless in KOH; clamp connections absent
NAME ORIGIN  means 'brown'
SIMILAR  Leccinum fibrillosum can be confused according to Thiers, but cut flesh turns fuscous with an intermediate reddish phase, the cap is normally more red-brown and more fibrillose, and growth is under conifers. See also SIMILAR section of Leccinum scabrum.
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Thiers(13), Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum clavatum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 135. 1966
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include a whitish to tan, somewhat viscid cap, white flesh that stains directly bluish gray when cut, whitish pores that bruise brown, a club-shaped stem with scabers that slowly become dull brown, and growth under conifers.^ RANGE Leccinum clavatum is known only from the Pacific Northwest and the northern Rocky Mountains, (Bessette). The type from ID, and it also occurs in CA, (Both).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  8-20cm, pallid becoming crust-brown; dry becoming subviscid [somewhat viscid], appressed fibrillose when young, (Smith(4)), whitish to tan, with band of sterile tissue on the margin, (Bessette)^, 8-20cm, "convex becoming broadly convex, margin appendiculate as in L. aurantiacum", cap margin often intergrown with stem; white to pallid ("tilleul-buff"), when young, gradually becoming pale tan ("pinkish-buff" to "cinnamon-buff") on disc or over all when old; "typically dry, when old and moist somewhat viscid, appressed fibrillose-tomentose", the fibrils becoming aggregated into small appressed scales over all when old, areolate [cracked like dried mud], (Smith(38))
FLESH  white, staining directly bluish gray in young specimens when cut^, (Smith), white, slowly stains fuscous without a reddish phase when exposed, (Bessette), 2-3cm thick, soft and spongy; white, turning bluish gray in young specimens when cut, unchanging in old caps; in stem white "except for the yellowish to greenish-yellow base in old specimens", (Smith(38))
PORES  2 per mm, whitish at first, bruising dingy yellowish then dingy brown^, (Smith(4)), 2 per mm, round, not stuffed, with thin walls; "whitish, gradually yellowish or if bruised sordid olive-yellow, when young quickly changing to brownish where bruised", tube layer 1-2cm thick, "depressed, whitish when young, soon dingy avellaneous, changing to brownish where bruised", (Smith(38))
STEM  4-8cm x 1.5-3cm, clavate, up to 7cm thick at base, white with scabers that slowly become dull brown^, (Smith(4)), 4-8cm x 1.5-3cm, bulbous base up to 7cm wide, solid; 'white at first, densely scabrous, scabrosity slowly darkening to dull brown, in old specimens surface may appear coarsely fibrillose with the fibrils toward the base dark brown, staining blue where cut or handled ("light methyl blue")', note young buttons did not give this change but in time changed to pinkish, (Smith(38))
ODOR  faintly fragrant^ (Smith(38))
TASTE  mild to nutty^ (Smith(38))
EDIBILITY  
HABITAT  type gregarious under spruce and fir^, (Smith(38))
SPORE DEPOSIT  dark rusty brown^ ("Mars brown"), (Smith(38))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-18(19) x 4-5 microns^, end cells of cap cuticle tubular to narrowly clavate; caulocystidia clavate, mucronate or fusoid-ventricose, all mixed together, (Smith(4)), spores 14-18(19) x 4-5 microns, narrowly subfusoid, smooth, yellowish or a few reddish tawny in Melzer's reagent, yellowish transparent to dingy ochraceous revived in KOH, wall relatively thin; basidia 4-spored, 28-34 x 8-10 microns, very delicate and soon collapsing; pleurocystidia "scattered, 34-46 x 8-12 microns, fusoid-ventricose with tapered curved neck and subacute apices", colorless to yellowish or darker as revived, smooth, thin-walled, cheilocystidia "similar to pleurocystidia but neck less elongated"; caulocystidia 36-57 x 10-16(20) microns, clavate to fusoid-ventricose or mucronate, content pale ochraceous, walls smooth and thin, caulobasidia [basidia on the stem] and basidioles "18-33 x 7-12 microns, content ochraceous, often merely in the form of unenlarged hyphal tips but mostly clavate"; cap cutis a matted down aggregation of fibrils with content yellowish to yellowish-brown when revived in KOH and yellowish transparent in Melzer's reagent, the end-cells 5-15 microns wide and tubular to narrowly clavate, hyphal cells tending to disarticulate in mounts from old caps, walls smooth and thin; hyphae of context 10-15(20) microns wide, interwoven, yellowish transparent to pale orange when revived in Melzer's reagent; clamp connections none, (Smith(38))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'clavate' or 'club-shaped', referring to stem
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Smith(38), Smith(4), Bessette(3), Both(1)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum cyaneobasileucum  Lannoy & Estades^  Docums Mycol. 21(no. 81): 23. 1991;  Leccinum brunneogriseolum  Lannoy & Estades
ENGLISH NAMES  bluing birch bolete
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark grayish brown to tan-colored cap that is suedelike on the surface, 2) flesh that does not turn blue above the stem base, 3) pore surface that is creamy white to beige when young with small pores, the tubes depressed, 4) a club-shaped whitish stem that is densely covered with small plaques and scabers at first that gradually blacken, the stem base often with blue-green blotches and slowly bluing when scraped, and 5) growth with birches, often in urban areas. RANGE Leccinum cyaneobasileucum is found in CA (Siegel(2)) and also occurs in the Pacific Northwest at least in OR, (N. Siegel, pers. comm.). It was described from France and occurs elsewhere in Europe including (Buczacki(1)) Britain/Ireland.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  KOH orange on cap, yellow in stem flesh
CAP  4-12cm across, "hemispheric at first, soon bun shaped, to broadly convex", sometimes uplifted with a wavy margin when old; dark gray to grayish brown or tan, "sometimes paler beige", "usually with a narrow white band around margin"; "moist to dry, suedelike to finely tomentose, often bumpy and rough at first, becoming nearly smooth"^, (Siegel), 5-16cm, hazel-brown, fulvous-brown, or walnut-brown; dry but may be tacky in wet weather, (Phillips)
FLESH  solid; whitish marbled with beige, unchanging^, (Siegel), "white, flushing pale pink, sometimes blue-green" (Phillips), "soft, unchanging or becoming slightly pinkish in cap and stem apex", usually "with bluish staining in base and where damaged by snails or insects", (Buckzacki)
PORES  "round, tiny at first", staying small when old; creamy white to beige when young, "soon pale buff", dingy when old; tubes deeply depressed at stem, relatively short at first, becoming long^, (Siegel), pores small, angular, white-gray, bruising brownish; tubes white-grayish (Buczacki)
STEM  5-15cm x 1-3cm, cylindric "with a swollen, club-shaped base, usually tall and straight"; white to creamy white, "but covered in small plaques and scabers" which are "very pale and dense at first but soon become light gray to dusky gray and then nearly black and more spread out" as the stem elongates, base "often with bright teal to blue-green blotches, and slowly bluing when scraped"^, (Siegel), 7-20cm x 0.8-2.0cm (Phillips), the dense covering of white woolly scales may later turn grayish and cause the entire stem to appear woolly (Buczacki)
ODOR  indistinct
TASTE  mild
EDIBILITY  edible, "and with a good flavor, but often quite slimy in texture", (Siegel)
HABITAT  single or scattered in troops, "sometimes in large numbers on urban lawns under planted birches"; in California Redwood Coast frequently found with Russula versicolor, Paxillus cuprinus, Lactarius pubescens var. betulae, and Cortinarius hemitrichus, and common in late summer and early fall^, (Siegel), with birch in moss, but not normally in Sphagnum, summer to fall, (Phillips), on soil and peat on heathland, moorland and other acidic sites with birch, sometimes among mosses although often in drier habitats and not usually with Sphagnum, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  dusky brown^ (Siegel), walnut brown (Phillips)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 11-21 x 3.5-7 microns, "spindle-shaped, smooth"
NAME ORIGIN  
SIMILAR  Leccinum scabrum also occurs under urban birches but "has a dark brown cap when young and darker, denser scabers, and lacks the blue-green stains on the stipe", (Siegel(2)).
SOURCES  Siegel(2)*, Buczacki(1)*, Phillips(3)* (including the white variation)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum discolor  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 152-153. 1966
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry to subviscid cap that is orange brown and nearly bald, 2) white flesh that changes to pinkish then blackish, 3) white pores that become pale olive-brown and when bruised stain dark brown, 4) a white stem with conspicuous scabers that turn from whitish to dark brown or black, 5) growth under pine and aspen, and 6) microscopic characters including the absence of orange-brown pigment globules in the cap cuticle hyphae.^ Leccinum discolor is one of the species previously identified as L. aurantiacum (Theirs(13)). RANGE It is found from eastern Canada west to ID, MT, WY, CO, and CA, (Bessette). It has been reported from BC (M. Beug, pers. comm.).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  7-15cm, hemispheric to convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat; "dull orange-brown to orange-cinnamon or orange-yellow", becoming dull cinnamon when old; dry to subviscid, nearly bald; margin with flaps of sterile tissue^, (Bessette), 10-15cm, convex becoming broadly convex to flat-convex to depressed on disc to occasionally irregular in outline; brown to orange-red to sometimes pale buff; "dry to subviscid, dull, often shallowly pitted or reticulate", bald "but often appearing tomentose or fibrillose", sometimes cracked-areolate [cracked like dried mud] near margin; margin with typical sterile flaps, (Thiers(1))
FLESH  "white when exposed, staining pinkish to brownish orange, then fuscous"^, (Bessette), 1-3cm thick, white when first exposed, changing to flesh color to pink then blackish, (Thiers(1))
PORES  1-2 per mm, angular; "white at first, becoming greenish yellow to pale olive-brown", "staining dark brown when bruised"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, becoming depressed near stem when mature^, (Bessette), 0.5-1mm, angular, colored as tubes; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed, white when young, becoming pale olive-brown when mature, staining dark brown when bruised, (Thiers(1))
STEM  6-12cm x 1-3cm, nearly equal or widening downward, solid; with conspicuous scabers that are whitish when young and dark brown to black when old over whitish ground color; dry; basal mycelium white; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 6-12cm x 1-3cm at top, equal to club-shaped, solid; white to whitish ground color, conspicuously ornamented, squamules white to pallid when young, becoming dark brown to black when old; dry; white mycelium at base, (Thiers(1))
ODOR  mild^ (Thiers(1))
TASTE  mild^ (Thiers(1))
EDIBILITY  reported by Theirs(1) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  single, scattered or in groups on ground under pine and aspen^, (Bessette), single to scattered in soil under aspen (Thiers(13))
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette, Thiers)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 15-20 x 4.5-6.5 microns, fusoid [spindle-shaped] to subcylindric [nearly cylindric], smooth, some producing a dextrinoid reaction, ochraceous^; cap cuticle hyphae lacking orange-brown pigment globules when revived in Melzer's reagent, (Bessette), spores 15.5-20 x 4.5-6.5 microns, fusoid to subcylindric, smooth, ochraceous in KOH and Melzer's reagent, some showing a dextrinoid reaction, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 30-37 x 9-12 microns, clavate to pear-shaped, colorless and with granular contents in KOH; hymenial cystidia 30-46 x 8-15 microns, "fusoid to clavate with elongated, tapering apices", colorless to sometimes appearing filled with dark brown amorphous material in KOH, thin-walled; cap cuticle a tangled trichodermium of free hyphal tips, buff in KOH, ochraceous tawny in Melzer's reagent, "hyphae 8-10 microns wide, walls smooth to asperulate and sometimes appearing incrusted, considerable disarticulation of cells noted, terminal cells only slightly narrowed at apex"; caulocystidia 25-45 x 10-17 microns, "clavate to mucronate to subfusoid", mostly dark brown in KOH but some colorless cells present, "an occasional large cell interspersed", walls smooth, thin; clamp connections absent, (Thiers(1))
NAME ORIGIN  means "of a different color", (Both(1))
SIMILAR  Leccinum aurantiacum has an abundance of pigment globules formed within the cells of the cap cuticle, whereas in L. discolor the contents remain homogeneous or only become finely granular, (Thiers). Leccinum insigne does not have a reddish phase in the discoloration of the exposed flesh, (Thiers).
SOURCES  Thiers(1), Bessette(3)*, Thiers(13), Both(1), Desjardin(6)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum fallax  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 146. 1966
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark red to ferruginous-red cap that is dry and appears fibrillose under a hand lens, 2) white flesh that slowly turns dingy vinaceous then dark gray when cut and that stains blue-green in the stem base, 3) pallid to grayish pores that bruise slightly yellowish to green then brownish, 4) a conspicuously club-shaped - bulbous stem that is white with scabers gradually becoming brownish to dark brown but not black, and 5) microscopic characters including pigment balls that form in Melzer's reagent.^ RANGE The type was found in ID.
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  
CAP  6-20cm, convex, dark red to ferruginous-red; dry, matted-fibrillose^, (Smith(4)), appears fibrillose only when examined under a lens (Bessette), 6-20cm, obtuse to convex expanding to broadly convex, margin appendiculate from sterile parts of overgrown cap margin; evenly rufous overall; dry, matted-fibrillose under hand lens, (Smith(38))
FLESH  thick; white, slowly staining dingy vinaceous then gray when cut^, (Smith(4)), thick, soft; "white slowly staining dingy grayish vinaceous where bruised or cut", in stem white, "staining reddish slowly when cut and finally going to blackish", staining bluish-green in base when damaged, (Smith(38))
PORES  "pallid to grayish, when bruised slightly staining yellowish to green and then brownish"^, (Smith(4)), small, round; "pallid to grayish staining slowly when bruised to yellowish green and then brownish or at times merely olive-yellowish"; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed around stem, "whitish, slowly becoming pale avellaneous, finally near wood-brown", (Smith(38))
STEM  5-14cm x 1-4cm near top, short-clavate with base up to 8cm wide; "white at first, ornamentation gradually becoming brownish to dark brown finally but not black"^, (Smith(4)), 6-14cm x 1-4cm, clavate [club-shaped] bulb up to 8cm wide, becoming nearly equal when old, solid; white, staining bluish-green around the base when handled or damaged; stem surface "scabrous-roughened, the scabrosity gradually staining dark brownish but not blackish even when dried", (Smith(38))
ODOR  mild^ (Smith(38))
TASTE  mild^ (Smith(38))
EDIBILITY  orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  type gregarious under spruce^, (Smith(38))
SPORE DEPOSIT  
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-17 x 3.2-4.5 microns^, cap cuticle of hyphae having content aggregating into globules when mounted in Melzer's reagent, end-cells subcylindric to narrowly clavate; caulocystidia up to 20 microns broad, mostly clavate, content colorless to yellowish in KOH, (Smith(4)), spores 14-17 x 3.2-4.5 microns, narrowly subfusoid, smooth, pale to bright tan in Melzer's reagent, yellowish in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 26-37 x 8-10 microns; pleurocystidia scattered, 44-60 x 9-15 microns, fusoid-ventricose, obtuse to subacute, colorless or with ochraceous content, smooth, thin-walled, cheilocystidia 18-30 x 5-8 microns, clavate to subfusoid, dull ochraceous in KOH; caulocystidia mostly clavate and 9-14 microns wide but some up to 20 microns wide, mostly not with a projecting neck, content colorless to yellowish in KOH, walls thin and smooth; cap cutis of appressed hyphae 4-11 microns wide, with dull orange-brown content in KOH, in Melzer's reagent the content orange-cinnamon to orange-red and aggregating into pigment balls, cells not or only slightly constricted at the septa, "end-cell nearly equal in width throughout or narrowly clavate to a short-tapered apex, walls thin and many hyphae with asperulate walls"; context hyphae orange to red when revived in Melzer's; all hyphae inamyloid; clamp connections absent, (Smith(38)) 
NAME ORIGIN  means 'false'
SIMILAR  
SOURCES  Smith(38), Smith(4), Bessette(3)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum fibrillosum  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Mich. Bot. 5: 152-153. 1966
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dry cap that is dark reddish brown or liver brown, conspicuously fibrillose at first becoming finely scaly, 2) white flesh that turns pinkish then purplish, 3) pores that are dingy buff when young and bruise yellow-brown, 4) a stem with dark blackish brown scabers, and 5) growth under conifers.^ Leccinum fibrillosum is found in the Rocky Mountains west to WA and north to western Canada, (Bessette(3)). RANGE Distribution includes eastern WA, ID, MT, WY, CO, and AB, (Bessette(1)).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh greenish blue with application of FeSO4, (Bessette(3))
CAP  6-25cm, obtuse to convex to flat, margin with band of sterile tissue; dark reddish brown fibrils and fine scales on paler reddish brown ground; dry^, (Bessette(3)), dry to sticky in moist weather; margin appendiculate with 1-2mm of sterile tissue, (Bessette(1)), 8-25cm, convex; dark liver brown; conspicuously fibrillose at first, changing from matted fibrillose to finely scaly, at times with overtone of grayish fibrils, (Smith)
FLESH  white, immediately flushing pink when exposed then changing to dull bluish red to bluish purple or fuscous^, (Bessette(3)), firm; white, immediately flushing pink in cap and upper stem when cut or bruised, "then changing to muddy bluish red to bluish purple or fuscous in 3 to 4 minutes, often nearly black after several minutes", (Bessette(1)), staining vinaceous then purple-drab (Smith)
PORES  1-2 per mm when mature, round to angular, buff to pale olivaceous, when old becoming olive-brown; staining brown when bruised; tube layer 0.5-1.7cm thick^, (Bessette(3)), at first 3-4 per mm, than 1-2mm when old, (Bessette(1)), dingy buff when young, staining yellow-brown where slightly injured, (Smith)
STEM  7-11cm x 2.5-4.5cm, nearly equal or narrowing or widening downward, solid; dark blackish brown scabers on white ground; dry; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette(3)), 4-12cm x 2-5cm, "densely coated with woolly-scabrous material that forms squamules that blacken", (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette(3))
EDIBILITY  reported by some authors including Bessette(3) as edible, but orange Leccinum species have been implicated in poisoning episodes
HABITAT  scattered or grouped under conifers, especially Engelmann Spruce and Lodgepole Pine^, (Bessette(3)), late summer and early fall (Miller)
SPORE DEPOSIT  olive-brown^ (Bessette(3))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-18 x 3.5-5 microns, elliptic, smooth, yellow-brown^; caulocystidia fusiform to ovate, and often pointed, (Bessette(3)), spores 14-18(20) x 3.8-5 microns, cap cuticle of tubular hyphae 6-12 microns wide, end cells tubular to weakly cystidioid, content of cells not forming globules in Melzer's; caulocystidia mostly ovate-pointed, (Smith), spores 14-18 x 3.8-5 microns, elliptic, yellow-brown in Melzer's reagent and KOH, thin-walled; basidia 4-spored, 23-36 x 8-11 microns, clavate, thin-walled; pleurocystidia and cheilocystidia 35-43 x 7-8.5 microns, fusiform to fusoid-ventricose, colorless to dark yellow-brown in KOH, thin-walled; cap cuticle a trichodermium of erect or tangled hyphae 6-12 microns wide, end cells somewhat cystidioid; caulocystidia 23-60 x 8-18 microns, thin-walled, fusiform to ovate, often pointed, (Bessette(1))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'fibrillose'
SIMILAR  Leccinum brunneum can be confused with this species but it has a dull, dark brown cap that is not strongly fibrillose, its flesh turns fuscous with no intervening reddish phase, and it grows under aspen, (Thiers(1)).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Bessette(1)*, Miller(14)*, Smith(4), Thiers(1)

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum holopus  (Rostk.) Watling^  Trans. Brit. Mycol. Soc. 43: 692. 1960
ENGLISH NAMES  pale rough-stem, white birch-bolete, white bog-bolete, ghost bolete
NOTES  Features include a whitish cap that is bald and may be viscid, white unchanging flesh, whitish pores that may bruise yellowish or brownish, a whitish stem with scabers that become brown, and bog habitat.^ The description here is for var. holopus: var. americanus is nearly identical but the cap is white at first, soon becoming dull vinaceous buff, with white context that changes to reddish when cut, (Bessette). RANGE The distribution of var. holopus as eastern Canada south to New York, west to the northern Rocky Mountains, of var. americanus eastern Canada south to NY, west to MI, (Bessette). L. holopus has been reported from BC and NB (collections at the University of British Columbia), ID (M. Beug, pers. comm.), AK and VT (collections at University of Washington), NS (Grund), and Europe (Breitenbach).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  cap cuticle displays a pink flash lasting less than one second, then negative, with application of KOH, flesh stains slightly olive with application of FeSO4^, (Bessette)
CAP  3-10cm, acutely convex, becoming broadly convex to nearly flat when old; "white or whitish when young, occasionally with gray, buff, tan, or pinkish tints", often darkening when old age and developing a greenish tinge; often viscid when moist or when old, bald or nearly so; margin with narrow sterile band of tissue^, (Bessette), 3-10cm, cushion-shaped to convex, becoming broadly convex or nearly flat; "dull white and remaining this color or if water-soaked olivaceous along a broad marginal area, disc often tinged buff to vinaceous-buff, rarely pea-green overall in age"; soft, subviscid, when old or wet slightly more viscid, typically bald and unpolished "but some slightly areolate at times over disc, rarely with inconspicuous streaks from appressed fibrils"; margin often exceeding tubes by 0.05cm, (Smith)
FLESH  "white, unchanging when cut or rubbed"^, (Bessette), thick, soft, white, when cut not staining or finally brownish (especially around larval tunnels); in stem not staining pink in apex when cut, but greenish to bluish in the stem base when mature, (Smith), take note of different staining reaction of var. americanus under NOTES
PORES  2-3 per mm, round; "whitish to slightly grayish or pale dingy brown, unchanging or staining yellowish or brownish when cut or bruised"; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick^, (Bessette), whitish becoming brownish, "when bruised staining yellow and then brown"; tube layer 1-2.5cm thick, adnate but soon deeply depressed around stem, pallid to pale olive-buff, when old wood-brown, (Smith)
STEM  8-14cm x 1-2cm, equal or widening slightly downward, solid; whitish ground color with scabers that are whitish when young and darken to tan or darker when old, "occasionally with greenish stains on the lower portion"; partial veil and annulus absent^, (Bessette), 8-14cm x 1-2cm, equal or nearly so; pallid, ornamented with squamules that become brownish (rarely blackish), top merely scurfy and remaining pallid a long time, (Smith)
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Smith)
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Smith)
EDIBILITY  yes (Bessette)
HABITAT  single to scattered on ground in and around bogs, cedar swamps or wet birch woods^, (Bessette), scattered to single in cold bogs, cedar swamps, etc., (Smith), summer to fall, (Buczacki)
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^ (Bessette), cinnamon-brown (Smith)
MICROSCOPIC  spores 14-20 x 5-6.5 microns, subfusoid [somewhat spindle-shaped], smooth, pale brown^, (Bessette), spores 14-20 x 5-6.5 microns, subfusoid, smooth, yellowish in Melzer's reagent when immature and dark reddish brown when mature, pale dingy cinnamon to ochraceous-cinnamon revived in KOH, no apical pore seen, wall slightly thickened; basidia 4-spored, 26-32 x 8-11 microns, clavate with colorless to pale cinnamon content in KOH, yellowish in Melzer's reagent; pleurocystidia scattered to rare, almost embedded in hymenium, 28-36 x 9-12 microns, fusoid-ventricose, content not distinctive; cap cuticle a trichodermium of interwoven subgelatinous hyphae 4-7 microns wide, "end-cells tubular to somewhat cystidioid with obtuse apex"; caulocystidia "mostly the fusoid-ventricose type with elongate somewhat flexuous necks and subacute apex", clavate cells also present; clamp connections absent, (Smith)
NAME ORIGIN  from Latin meaning "with perfect stalk", (Schalkwijk-Barendsen)
SIMILAR  Leccinum rotundifoliae has a white to tan or grayish tan cap that becomes pinkish buff to cinnamon-buff or darker (as opposed to L. holopus which has a cap that is whitish to grayish when young, darkening when old and often developing a greenish or olive tinge), (Bessette).
SOURCES  Bessette(3)*, Smith(35), Phillips(1)*, Schalkwijk-Barendsen(1)* (var. americanum), Courtecuisse(1)*, Breitenbach(3)*, Grund(11), Buczacki(1)*

FAMILY  Boletaceae, Order Boletales, Class Agaricomycetes, Phylum Basidiomycota
LATIN NAMES  Leccinum idahoense  A.H. Sm., Thiers, and Watling^  Lloydia 31: 256-257. 1968
ENGLISH NAMES  
NOTES  Features include 1) a dark brown viscid granular cracked cap, 2) whitish flesh that becomes vinaceous gray in 15 minutes, 3) small whitish to olive-buff pores that bruise brown, 4) dingy cinnamon-buff scabers that become blackish in the lower half of the stem, and 5) relatively pale cinnamon spore deposit.^ RANGE Leccinum idahoense is found in ID, but "distribution limits remain to be established", (Bessette).
CHEMICAL REACTIONS  ^flesh slightly greenish with FeSO4, (Smith(37))
CAP  8-15cm, convex, margin incurved at first, narrow band of marginal tissue breaks into segments when old; uniform dark brown; "viscid, granulose-areolate"^, (Bessette), 8-15cm, broadly convex; dark liver-brown; granulose-areolate (like 'Boletus' mirabilis), very viscid even in dry weather; "sterile margin breaking into small segments but definitely forming a sterile band at first", (Smith(37)), 8-13(20)cm, dark liver brown; viscid, granulose-roughened, (Smith(4))
FLESH  "whitish, very slowly staining vinaceous gray (taking up to 15 minutes) when exposed"^, (Bessette), pallid, when cut unchanging or in top of stem slowly slightly vinaceous gray in 15 minutes, with a watery line above tubes when mature, (Smith(37)), pallid, when cut turning faintly vinaceous-gray in about 15 minutes, (Smith(4))
PORES  2-3 per mm, round to angular, whitish becoming pale olive-buff when mature, bruising brown; tube layer 1-2cm thick, depressed near stem when old^, (Bessette), up to 3 per mm; pale olive-buff when mature, where bruised "severely changing to vinaceous brown or finally to wood-brown, where bruised slightly staining olive"; tube layer about 2cm thick, depressed, pallid when young, slowly changing to vinaceous gray on cutting, (Smith(37)), "small and staining olive when lightly bruised, pale olive buff when young", (Smith(4))
STEM  7-12cm x 2-3.5cm, widening downward or nearly equal, with constricted base, stem solid; "whitish, with dingy cinnamon-buff scabers that become blackish, especially near the base, in age"; dry; partial veil and ring absent^, (Bessette), 8-12cm x 2.5-3.5cm in upper part, solid, equal, flared at top in some, more or less clavate [club-shaped] when young, appearing pinched off at base; pallid, with dingy cinnamon-buff ornamentation in upper part, finally nearly blackish in lower part, "exterior at base stained greenish", (Smith(37)), 8-12cm x 2.5-3.5cm, pinched off at base, scabers "cinnamon buff but finally blackish over lower half", (Smith(4))
ODOR  not distinctive^ (Bessette), mild (Smith(37))
TASTE  not distinctive^ (Bessette), mild (Smith(37))
EDIBILITY  unknown (Bessette)
HABITAT  single or scattered on ground under conifers^, (Bessette), under western cedar, hemlock, and western white pine mixed, (Smith(4))
SPORE DEPOSIT  brown^, (Bessette), dingy cinnamon when air-dried (Smith(4)), "Sayal-brown" (Ridgway color) when moist, pale cinnamon after loss of moisture as in most species of Suillus, (Smith(37))
MICROSCOPIC  spores 13-17 x 4-5 microns, subfusoid (somewhat spindle-shaped), smooth, dull ocher-yellow^, (Bessette), spores 13-16(17) x 3.8-5.2 microns, subfusoid, smooth, nearly colorless in Melzer's reagent "but many becoming bright cinnamon overall or only in the distal portion", dull ocher-yellow in KOH; basidia 4-spored, 22-30 x 10-15 microns, clavate, colorless revived in KOH and with large oil droplet; pleurocystidia scattered, 38-52 x 9-16 microns, "ventricose with slightly enlarged apex above a narrowly tapered neck, or obtusely fusoid-ventricose, with smoky brown content when revived in KOH"; cap cuticle a tangled turf of hyphae 4-7(10) microns wide, "with cells often short but not enlarged, in KOH or Melzer's filled with orange-brown droplets of pigment, end-cells tubular with obtuse apex to somewhat cystidioid but not conspicuously inflated, many hyphae with disarticulating cells"; caulocystidia 24-42 x 8-15 microns, "fusoid-ventricose, thin-walled, the content smoky brown in KOH", caulocystidioles 20-25 x 8-12 microns, "not greatly enlarged, with smoky brown content and thin smooth walls"; clamp connections not seen, (Smith(37)), 13-16(17) x 3.8-5.2 microns; hyphae of cap cuticle 4-7(10) microns wide, filled with orange-brown globules in Melzer's, (Smith(4))
NAME ORIGIN  means 'from Idaho'
SIMILAR  Leccinum aurantiacum differs from L. idahoense in the latter's dark liver-brown granulose-areolate cap, its very slow change to gray when the flesh is cut, and its relatively pale cinnamon spore deposit, (Smith(37)).
SOURCES  Smith(37), Bessette(3), Smith(4) (as L. idahoensis)
